==PAGE:3==
Abstract
Weel & Sandvig is a Danish company that is currently developing a new high-speed industrial heat pump for
waste heat recovery. The heat pump is a centrifugal compressor that uses water as working ﬂuid. The de-
velopment of the heat pump is a response to increased interests for industrial waste heat recovery, a shifting
landscape of refrigerants and as well to reduce the fossil fuel consumption. The objective of this thesis is to
create two types of compressor maps of the high-speed heat pump that is under development at Weel & Sandvig.
One corrected to standard conditions and another corrected to standard conditions relative to the design point
values. The compressor maps should be based on a test rig results, having water vapor as a working ﬂuid.
All of the results lead to the creation of the compressor maps. Therefore, are the main results the compressor
maps and associated analysis of those maps. The pair of compressor maps were built based on the experimental
data. The corrected compressor map and the corrected relative compressor map were successfully validated.
Problems with the cooling system results in a lack of suﬃcient amount of usable data.
This could have a
negative impacts on the accuracy of new data added to the compressor maps.
A smoothed version of the
compressor maps was generated. Although the errors are expected when generating a smoothed version, the
overall accuracy in comparison to the original compressor maps is acceptable.
ii
==PAGE:4==
Preface
This thesis completes a master’s degree in Sustainable Energy, Thermal track, from the Department of Me-
chanical Engineering at Danmarks Tekniske Universitet. The master thesis is equivalent to 30 ECTS-credits.
Instructors are Andrea Meroni, Benjamin Zühlsdorf, Brian Elmegaard, Jens Mikkelsen and Mogens Weel. The
aim of this thesis is to create compressor maps of a high-speed heat pump from experimental data that is under
development at Weel & Sandvig.
Copenhagen, June 30, 2017.
Sigurður Kristinn Jóhannesson

Technical University of Denmark for their professional guidance and excellent suggestions.
Moreover, I am grateful to Jens Mikkelsen and Mogens Weel, my supervisors at Weel & Sandvig, for their guid-
ance and for providing me with the special opportunity of being part of the high-speed heat pump development
and learning so much about the topic in the process.
I want to thank my family, friends and the guys at Karma’s. You deserve a round of applause for keeping me
motivated and for providing good advices.

==PAGE:11==
1
Introduction
This chapter aims to explain the motivation 1.1 behind the project and then the objectives 1.2 of the project.
This should give the reader a picture of what is being done and why. Organization of the Thesis 1.4 is outlined
after the objects. An introduction is given to the state of art 1.5 to help the reader to obtain appropriate
knowledge about the compressors, compressor maps, heat pumps, isentropic eﬃciency, and refrigerants in order
to follow the method and results. Moreover, the state of the art will give the reader a deeper insight into the
motivation behind the project and to the objectives of the thesis.
1.1
Motivation
We face many global challenges in the 21st century that needs to be solved. A reliable and sustainable energy
supply, prevention of global warming and environmental protection are some among of them. As of mid-2015,
most countries around the world have adopted some sort of a renewable energy target to achieve these goals [1].
These targets provide a mechanism to shift economies on a more sustainable path. The renewable energy targets
take various forms between countries. According to a study done by the International Renewable Energy Agency
(IRENA), targets focused on renewable electricity are the most common one. However, in the last decade, there
is a considerable increase in heating/cooling and transport sector targets. As the targets have become more
widespread, they have played an important role in investment decision-making. With a smart policy making
and an impulsive energy support scheme, they shift the energy landscape towards a more renewable direction.
Heat pumps (HPs) have gained an important status in a world seeking improved energy eﬃciency, fossil fuel
consumption reduction and mitigating carbon dioxide (CO2) emissions. The heat pumps serve as a key compo-
nent within a renewable energy heating/cooling system and help to achieve set renewable energy targets [2]. A
compressor is commonly referred to as a heat pump in a heat pump and refrigeration cycles moves heat from
one location to another.
As mentioned before, heat pumps are an important technology in the endeavor to reduce CO2 emission and
improving energy eﬃciency. More importantly, when it comes to reaching heating/cooling targets, is the ap-
plication of industrial heat pumps (IHPs) that can play a large role in reaching those targets. The industry
is accountable for roughly a third of the total energy demand globally and CO2 emissions. In 2014, the total
primary energy supply was 13.699 Mtoe and the total CO2 global emissions were 32.381 Mt [3]. Nowadays, the
available low-temperature waste heat in manufacturing industry makes up around 25% of the total energy used
by it [2].
There are multiple ways of applying industrial heat pumps to all sorts of manufacturing processes and opera-
tions. IHPs catches the waste heat from processes, that serves as a heat source and upgrades the heat ﬂow by a
compression to a useful temperature. They have manifold utilization possibilities such as heating or preheating
in industrial processes, space heating, cooling in the industry and other purposes.
These processes can be
for example drying, washing, evaporation, and distillation. Industries in question are food, beverages, wood,
textiles and chemicals industries.
Currently, a waste heat recovery under 100 ◦C is considered in most cases manageable. However, a waste heat
recovery above 100 ◦C is considered to be a challenge with the current state of technology. That creates a prob-
lem because many industrial processes take place at higher temperature levels [2]. Before applying solutions to
a waste heat recovery above 100 ◦C, a further research and development (R&D) are needed. That includes the
research and development of industrial heat pumps at a high temperature and industrial heat pump integration
and the development high-temperature refrigerants [4] [5].
Moreover, the development of industrial heat pumps have to take into account the future outlook of refrigerants.
To explain further, refrigerants are a synonym for ﬂuids used to transfer thermal energy in a heat pump and
refrigeration cycle. Authorities around the world want to reduce the environmental danger caused by refrig-
erants. The biggest concerns about refrigerants are global warming impact and ozone depletion. A ban has
been issued on many types of environmentally harmful refrigerants along with stricter regulations of refrigerant
application [6].
1
==PAGE:12==
Application of the refrigerant R-718 or water/steam as a working ﬂuid for industrial heat pumps can come to
terms with the environmental demands and eﬀorts of waste heat recovery. Water is in the category of natural
refrigerant and is environmentally friendly. Water oﬀers many advantages as a refrigerant, it is completely
nontoxic to the environment, cost eﬃcient and easily obtained. Additionally, water is seen as promising refrig-
erants for heat pumps for various industrial application over the temperature range of 80-100◦C [7]. Another
advantage that application of water as a refrigerant oﬀers is the technology known as MVR or mechanical vapor
recompression. With MVR, the water vapor is compressed to a higher temperature and pressure and is directly
integrated into e.g. a drying process. The water vapor can be retrieved as a waste heat instead of a boiler
application and reapplied via compression. The MVR technology reduces the energy consumption of evaporated
water considerably [8]
Weel & Sandvig is a Danish company that is currently developing a new high-speed industrial heat pump for
waste heat recovery. The heat pump is a centrifugal compressor that uses water as working ﬂuid. The develop-
ment of the heat pump is a response to increased interests for industrial waste heat recovery, a shifting landscape
of refrigerants and as well to reduce the fossil fuel consumption. The compressor is still in development and
there are multiple tasks for the company have to be carried out. One of those tasks is to make a compressor
map of the compressor that is based on a compressor test rig results.
A compressor map helps to estimate the performance of a compressor under various conditions in a visible
manner and facilitates to see how a compressor meets criteria of industry projects.
Therefore, by making compressor map of a particular compressor its performance under various permissible
conditions can be estimated, even if those conditions have not been documented for the compressor before.
That is because a compressor map is made up by calculated data and interpolation.
2
==PAGE:13==
1.2
Objective of the project
The objective of this thesis is to create two diﬀerent compressor maps of the high-speed heat pump that is
under development at Weel & Sandvig. The compressor maps should be based on a test rig results, having
water vapor as a working ﬂuid.
The ﬁrst compressor map should be a corrected compressor map. Meaning that the compressor map should be
made up by corrected mass ﬂow rate, pressure ratio, corrected compressor speed and isentropic eﬃciency. A
correction of the mass ﬂow rate and the compressor speed is a common representation of the compressor map
by industry.
The other compressor map should be a corrected relative compressor map. Meaning that the compressor map
should be made up by corrected relative mass ﬂow rate, reduced relative pressure ratio, corrected relative
compressor speed and relative isentropic eﬃciency. The relativized compressor map is beneﬁcial for simulation
proposes of the same compressor for diﬀerent gases and inlet conditions.
These concepts of the compressor maps will be explained in more detail in the Compressor map section .
1.3
Limitation on the study
Study limitation are the following:
• Uncertainty is adherent with experimental data.
• Unrealistic to test for every compressor speed and valve position.
• Speeds under 31,500 rounds per minute were not included in the planning because those speeds are unlikely
operational speeds. Moreover, there is higher sensibility in the system and increasing error eﬀects at low
speeds.
• Diﬃculty collecting a good and suﬃcient amount of data due to the dysfunctional cooling system.
• Limited control over the throttle valve position. The valve position was set by given a pulse signal and
checking the valve position by visual observation on a gauge.
• Time is a limiting factor. The cooling system needed remediation and there was not enough time to wait
for that.
• Air content in the working ﬂuid cannot be measured.
1.4
Organization of the thesis
The report is organized as follows:
• Chapter 1 is Introduction. This chapter explains the project motivation, objectives, and gives an intro-
duction to the state of the art relevant to the project.
• Chapter 2 is Method. This chapter describes the test rig and methodology of the project.
• Chapter 3 is Results And Discussions. The chapter gives the analysis of the results.
• Chapter 4 is Conclusions And Recommendations. This chapter is dedicated to conclusions from the results,
fulﬁllment of the objectives and recommendations.
• Chapter 5 is Appendix. The chapter contains extra information that is relevant to the main subject matter
such as data tables, plots, and codes.

==PAGE:14==
1.5
State of the art
1.5.1
Turbomachinery
Turbomachines are classiﬁed as machines that transfer energy to a ﬂuid or extract energy from a ﬂuid. The
impacted ﬂuid moves continuously through the turbomachinery in a dynamic process. There are two main
subcategories to classify turbomachines depending upon whether they increase or decrease the energy of the
ﬂuid [9]. Those that add power to the ﬂuid are called pumps, compressors and blowers, depending on the state of
the ﬂuid and level of the pressure rise. Those that extract energy from the ﬂuid and lower its pressure are called
wind, hydraulic, steam or gas turbines. Normally both types are connected to a rotating shaft that transfers
energy to or from the turbomachinery. Moreover, turbomachines are divided into more categories depending
on the ﬂuid passage design. Those categories are axial ﬂow, radial ﬂow, and mixed ﬂow turbomachines [10].
Figure 1: Type of turbomachines [10].
The axial ﬂow turbomachines are shown as (a) and (e) in ﬁgure 1. What makes them categorized as axial ﬂow
turbomachines is due to fully or mainly parallel through-ﬂow of the ﬂuid to the axis of rotation. The radial-ﬂow
or a centrifugal turbomachine is shown as (c) in the same ﬁgure. In this case, the ﬂuid path in fully or mainly
perpendicular to the rotating axis. Figure 1 (b) and (d) show mixed turbomachines, a mixed ﬂow pump, and a
mixed ﬂow hydraulic turbine respectively. Additionally, turbomachines can be classiﬁed as impulse or reaction
turbomachines depending upon whether pressure changes occur or not. In part (f) of the same ﬁgure is the
Pelton wheel that is an impulse turbine. The ﬂuid is directed onto the rotor making the pressure change in
the process occurring in one or more nozzles [10]. In a comparison of compressor types, axial or centrifugal
(radial), an axial compressor is more compact and can show a slightly higher eﬃciency. Additionally, the axial
compressor has a three to four times smaller diameter for the same capacity and volumetric ﬂow in comparison.
Additionally, the axial compressors likely have higher eﬃciencies for higher ﬂow rates. On the other hand,
4
==PAGE:15==
centrifugal compressors have relatively higher pressure ratio per stage, and most likely a better eﬃciency at
lower ﬂow rates and well-documented design tools [5] [10]. Additionally, centrifugal compressors tend to have a
considerably lower invest costs [11].
1.5.2
Centrifugal compressors
This section seeks to give an introduction to centrifugal compressors. The high-speed turbo compressor being
researched in this thesis is a radial compressor or also known as a centrifugal compressor. Therefore is the
focus to explain the basic characteristics of the compressor, give an insight into the operation and basic design
parameters.
It is recorded that centrifugal turbomachines have been in use now for more than a century.
Centrifugal
compressors are practical in many applications such as in "small gas turbine for road vehicles and commercial
helicopters as well as bigger applications, e.g., diesel engine turbochargers, chemical plant processes, factory
workshop air supplies, and large-scale air-conditioning plant" [10].
In order to deﬁne a compressor, it is a
machine the increases the pressure of a ﬂowing gas and the density ratio across the compressor is 1.05 or more
[10].
Figure 2: A type of a centrifugal compressor applied by Sir Frank Whittle [10].
Figure 2 represents an example of a centrifugal compressor. The impeller lies at the heart of the centrifugal
compressor and has series of vanes mounted on it. The ﬂuid passage is between the impeller vanes, hence when
the impeller rotates the ﬂuid rotates with it. The energy level of the ﬂuid is increased by means of the impeller
that acts on the ﬂuid with angular momentum. Both the velocity and the static pressure level of the ﬂuid are
increased.
5
==PAGE:16==
Figure 3: An impeller stage and velocity diagram in a centrifugal compressor [10].
As seen in ﬁgure 3, the ﬂuid enters the into the eye at suction side. The diﬀuser is a non-rotating device that
has an increased ﬂow area over the ﬂow passage. The role of the diﬀuser is to convert the kinetic energy or
reducing the excess ﬂow velocity to increased pressure. The collector scroll collects the ﬂuid exiting the diﬀuser
and is the bridge to the outlet pipe. When the ﬂuid is ﬂowing through the channel, the hub (a-b) and the
shroud (c-d) form the outer boundaries of the ﬂuid passage. The inlet velocity of the ﬂuid is denoted as C1
and the outlet velocity is C2. The velocity can be divided into two parts, one being the radial velocity Cr and
the other is the tangential velocity Cθ. The torque that acts on the ﬂuid and alters its speed comes from the
impeller. The blade velocity or the velocity of the impeller vane is denoted as U1 at the entry and U2 at the
exit. Additionally, the relative ﬂuid velocity is denoted with W1 at entry and W2 at the exit. The relative ﬂuid
is diﬀerent between the absolute velocity of the ﬂuid C and the impeller’s vane velocity. These velocities form
the concept of velocity triangles that can help predict the approximate performance of a turbomachinery by
simpliﬁcation [10]. The torque required to be applied to the ﬂuid by the impeller is [12]:
T = ˙m ∗(r2 ∗cθ2 −r1 ∗cθ1)
(1)
Where T is the torque, ˙m is the mass ﬂow through the compressor, r2 is the radius of the impeller tip, cθ2 is
the tangential speed of the ﬂuid at impeller tip, r1 is the radius to the eye and cθ1 is the tangential speed of
the ﬂuid into the impeller.
Enthalpy is the sum of an internal energy of a substance plus the product of pressure and volume. Speciﬁc
enthalpy is an expression of enthalpy on a unit mass basis. Speciﬁc enthalpy is a measurement of the energy
content of a substance. A compressor does work on the ﬂuid passing through the compressor and increases the
energy content of the ﬂuid. The change in speciﬁc enthalpy ∆h over the compressor can be written as [10] [13]:
∆h = h2 −h1 = U2 ∗cθ2 −U1 ∗cθ1
(2)
Where ∆h is the change in speciﬁc enthalpy, h2 is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the compressor outlet, h1 is the
speciﬁc enthalpy at the compressor inlet, U2 is the blade speed at the impeller tip, cθ2 is the tangential speed
of the ﬂuid at impeller tip. U1 is the blade speed at the impeller entry and cθ1 is the tangential speed of the
ﬂuid at impeller entry. As shown in ﬁgure 3 the inlet tangential velocity of the ﬂuid is zero and therefore is the
angular momentum zero. The ﬂow enters freely axially into the impeller, this is the normal case in centrifugal
compressors and pumps.
By multiplying mass ﬂow with the speciﬁc enthalpy change equation 2, the work required by the impeller is de-
rived. Assuming adiabatic condition, meaning that the process has no thermal interaction with its surroundings,
the work added to the ﬂuid can be written as [10]:
P = ˙m ∗(h2 −h1)
(3)
Where P is the work done by the compressor, ˙m is the mass ﬂow passing through the compressor, h2 is the
speciﬁc enthalpy at the outlet and h1 is the enthalpy at the inlet.

==PAGE:17==
1.5.3
Compressor isentropic eﬃciency
This section deals with the isentropic eﬃciency of the compressor or in other words its performance. Evaluation
of the compressor’s performance lays a foundation for the creation of the compressor map.
An isentropic process is adiabatic and reversible, meaning that the process can return to initial state. On the
other hand, an actual process is irreversible and cannot reach the initial value from its ﬁnal value. Figure 4
shows the comparison of an actual and an isentropic compression. On the x-axis is speciﬁc entropy and on
the y-axis is speciﬁc enthalpy. The green curves are isobaric lines, meaning that the pressure is constant. The
isentropic compression process moves vertically upwards along the speciﬁc enthalpy scale, leaving a no change in
speciﬁc entropy. The speciﬁc enthalpy diﬀerence is h2s−h1. However, an actual compression process experiences
irreversibility and therefore the speciﬁc entropy of the working ﬂuid must increase to the right of the isentropic
endpoint but on the same isobaric line giving the same output pressure. The speciﬁc enthalpy diﬀerence is
h2 −h1 is larger than for an isentropic one, hence it requires more work done by the compressor [13].
Figure 4: Comparison of actual and isentropic compression [13].
The compressor isentropic eﬃciency is a ratio that measures how eﬀectively the actual compression process of a
compressor it to in regards to an isentropic process. Additionally, an isentropic eﬃciency is dimensionless and
is equal or less to one. The isentropic eﬃciency of a compressor can be written as [13]:
ηisen = Isentropic compressor work
Actual compressor work
= h2,s −h1
h2 −h1
(4)
Where ηisen is the isentropic eﬃciency, h2,s is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the outlet for the isentropic process,
h2 is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the outlet for the actual process and h1 is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the inlet.
The speciﬁc enthalpy is derived by temperature and pressure level before and after the compressor assuming
adiabatic process and corrections of the measured data.

1.5.4
Heat pump and refrigeration cycles
The compressor is designed to serve as a heat pump and therefore it is important to give a basic understand-
ing how heat pump and refrigeration operate. Heat pump and refrigeration system play an important role in
everyday life. For instance, are refrigeration cycles used e.g. for food preservation, air conditioning, and vari-
ous industrial processes. Heat pumps are e.g. used to heat up building and delivered heat to industrial processes.
In principle, a heat pump and refrigeration cycle absorb thermal energy from a heat source, lift the temperature
by adding external energy to the refrigerant via heat pumps and then releases thermal to a heat sink. This
is done by relying on the pressure-temperature relation of the refrigerant. The heat source is usually called
the evaporator and the heat sink is usually called a condenser in a vapor compression refrigeration system.
Fundamentally, the heat pump and refrigeration cycle operate in the same manner, therefore, the explanation
here below applies to both types of cycles.
(a) Simple cycle vapor-compressor heat pump cycle.
(b) T-s diagram.
Figure 5: A ﬁgure with two sub-ﬁgures [13]
Figure 5a shows an example of a simple refrigeration cycle [13] and ﬁgure 5b shows the same process with a
T-s diagram. In order to explain the main working principals of the cycle let us start at node 4. The working
ﬂuid crosses the evaporator and a heat transfer is transferred to the refrigerant under constant pressure. The
heat transfer of Qin is the heat source for a heat pump or a cooling load for a refrigeration cycle [13]. The heat
transfer is shown as [13]:
˙Qin = ˙m ∗(h1 −h4)
(5)
Where ˙Qin is the rate of heat transfer, ˙m is the mass ﬂow of the refrigerant and h1 is the speciﬁc enthalpy at
the evaporator’s outlet and h4 is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the evaporator’s inlet. At node 1 the refrigerant has
turned into a saturated or superheated vapor before entering the compressor. The refrigerant is compressed
by the compressor to a higher pressure and to an increased temperature. A pressure ratio is the ratio of the
outlet pressure at node 2 to the inlet pressure at node 1. Assuming adiabatic condition, the work done by the
compressor is [13]:
˙Wc = ˙m ∗(h2 −h1)
(6)
Where ˙Wc is the rate of work done by the compressor, ˙m is the mass ﬂow of the refrigerant, h2 is the speciﬁc
enthalpy at the outlet and h1 is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the inlet. The isentropic eﬃciency of the compressor is
calculated by the same method as previously introduced in the Compressor isentropic eﬃciency section 1.5.3.
The isentropic eﬃciency of a compressor can be written as [13]:
ηisen = h2,s −h1
h2 −h1
(7)
Where ηisen is the isentropic eﬃciency, h2,s is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the outlet for the isentropic process, h2
is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the outlet for the actual process and h1 is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the inlet. At node
2 the refrigerant enters the condenser to exchange thermal energy with a colder surrounding. The refrigerant
should be fully condensed or slightly subcooled after then condenser. The heat transfer from the system under
adiabatic process can be written as [13]:
8
==PAGE:19==
˙Qout = ˙m ∗(h2 −h3)
(8)
Where ˙Qout is the heat release, ˙m is the mass ﬂow through the condenser, h3 is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the
condenser’s outlet and h1 is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the condenser’s inlet. Between node 3 & 4 there is an
expansion value where the refrigerant expands until reaching the evaporator’s pressure. The process is assumed
to be reversible and adiabatic. The refrigerant keeps a constant speciﬁc enthalpy shown as [13]:
h3 = h4
(9)
Where h3 is the speciﬁc enthalpy before the expansion valve and h4 is the speciﬁc enthalpy after the expansion
valve. The coeﬃcient of performance (COP) is used to evaluate the performance of heat pump or refrigeration
cycles.
For a refrigeration cycle, the COP is the ratio of absorbed thermal energy (what you get) divided by external
energy added to the system (what you pay). Basically, the goal is to remove as much heat as possible through
the evaporator in the refrigeration cycle and then apply as little work as possible to the process. On the contrary,
in a heat pump cycle, the objective is to deliver as much heat as possible by using as little work as possible to
the process [13]. The COP for a refrigeration cycle can be written as [13]:
COPβ =

= h1 −h4
h2 −h1
(10)
Where COPβ is the coeﬃcient of performance in a refrigeration cycle, ˙Qin is the heat added to the system
and

Wc is compressor work done to the system. h1 is the speciﬁc enthalpy between the evaporator and the
compressor, h4 is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the evaporator inlet and h2 is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the compressor
outlet. The COP of the heat pump cycle observes that ratio of heat delivered to work input. The COP for a
heat pump cycle can be written as [13]:
COPγ =

= h2 −h3

(11)
Where COPγ is the coeﬃcient of performance in a heat pump cycle, ˙Qout is the heat delivered by the system
and

Wc is compressor work done to the system. h2 is the speciﬁc enthalpy between the condenser and the
compressor, h3 is the speciﬁc enthalpy at condenser’s outlet and h1 is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the compressor
inlet.
The Carnot coeﬃcient of performance (COPc) is the theoretical maximum coeﬃcient of performance a cycle
can achieve under a constant reservoir temperatures. The Carnot coeﬃcient of performance (COPc) is the
theoretical maximum coeﬃcient of performance a cycle can achieve under a constant reservoir temperatures.
The COPc,β for a refrigeration cycle can be written as [13]:
COPc,β =

Where COPc,β is the Carnot coeﬃcient of performance in a refrigeration cycle, TC is the temperature of the

Figure 6 demonstrate how a heat pump system is used in a real world application. The heat pump cycle is
used for a residential space heating in the ﬁgure. The system is installed with all the same main elements as
are applied in a simple refrigeration cycle and.
9
==PAGE:20==
Figure 6: Residential heat pump system with air as a refrigerant [13].
There are several reasons why the actual cycle is diﬀerent from the maximum theoretical cycle. Broadly speaking,
the heat transfers are not reversible, there has to be a suﬃcient temperature diﬀerence between the refrigerant
and the reservoirs to keep the heat exchangers at a practical size. The compression from state 1 to state 2
happens under a two-phase liquid-vapor mixture in the maximum theoretical cycle compared to superheating
steam in an actual compression process. This process is usually referred to as wet compression. However,
wet compression in real life application can damage the compressor due to liquid droplets that are in the
mixture. Therefore, in an actual system, dry compression is preferable because the refrigerant has evaporated
completely. Additionally, in an actual cycle, there is a throttling process from state 3 to state 4 compared to
a turbine expansion in the theoretical cycle [13]. In this chapter, a simple refrigeration cycle was explained,
however, it is good to keep in mind that there are many variations of refrigeration cycle are used for improved
performance or for special application.
10
==PAGE:21==
1.5.5
Industrial heat pumps
Fundamentally, moving from heat pumps to industrial heat pumps, the diﬀerence between the technologies lies
in the scale and the operational temperature level. Otherwise is the theoretical approach described in Heat pump
and refrigeration cycles 1.5.4 very similar. The residential heat pump market is already matured and oﬀering
standardized solutions, meanwhile, most IHPs applications require special adaption to a particular condition.
IHPs seem to have few advantages in comparison to HPs. The COP is high as a result of low-temperature lift.
The annual operating hours are higher. The economics of scale because of larger units and a tighter operat-
ing range between the reservoirs. The waste heat recovery and the heat demand use happen simultaneously.
However, in light of these pros, the number of IHPs installations is considerably lower than of HPs. The main
reasons for this appear to cause of limitation of available refrigerants working in the desired temperature range,
lack of proven success of IHPs application by industry and certainty, insuﬃcient knowledge about the process
IHPs by specialists [2].
Figure 7: Reservoirs in an industrial heat pump application [2].
Figure 7 demonstrates how the heat source provides a heat transfer to the IHP system. Thereafter the thermal
bridge between the heat sink and the heat source is gaped by a compression work. The compression work is
that gives the refrigerant in the system the necessary temperature lift. The heat is then transferred through
the condenser to the heat sink for various processes [4]. The process described here above is basically the same
as shown in ﬁgure 5a although the illustration is set up diﬀerently in this case. The main driver behind ﬁgure
7 is to give an idea in a visible manner what is the evaporator’s heat source and the condenser’s heat sink.
Moreover, a heat source means what kind of waste heat is being recovered via the evaporator and the heat sink
means what kind of processes the condenser is delivering heat to.
As mentioned in Motivation 1.1 the industrial heat pump potential is tremendous. Therefore, understanding
the usage of the energy and ﬁnding a more eﬃcient way of application is therefore very important. The price
development of electricity and fossil fuels in the last decade has been positive for heat pump systems to progress
in most countries [14].
11
==PAGE:22==
Figure 8: Temperature levels in industrial heating applications in Australia [2].
Figure 8 displays a temperature operating range of various industrial sectors based on the most important
processes. This overview is helpful in order to further understand the potential of IHPs. As seen in the ﬁgure
there are thermal processes in each main category that operates around 80-100 ◦C and above, a temperate level
suitable for high-temperature IHPs. Additionally, in Denmark alone, studies show that about 25% of the energy
used in industry can be replaced by means of industrial heat pumps [5].
1.5.6
Water as a refrigerant
Refrigerants are a synonym for ﬂuids used to transfer thermal energy in a heat pump and refrigeration cycle.
Typically, the refrigerants are condensed and vaporized back and forth in such cycles.
In the last decades, the landscape of refrigerants has changed drastically as a result of ozone depletion and
greenhouse eﬀect caused be refrigerants when released into the atmosphere. Authorities around the world want
to reduce the environmental danger caused by refrigerants. Their biggest concerns about refrigerants are global
warming impact and ozone depletion. As a result, a ban has been issued on many types of environmentally
harmful refrigerants along with stricter regulations of refrigerant application [6]. This will call for new challenges
in research and development in other types of refrigerants along with integrating suitable oil and systems. There
are several concerns that refrigerants of the future must come to terms with. Those are appropriate thermo-
dynamics properties, zero potential of ozone depletion potential (ODP), appropriate global warming potential
(GWP), ﬂammability, toxicity, and costs [6]. A zero ozone depletion potential, meaning that the refrigerant will
not contribute to depletion of the ozone layer. The global warming potential is a factor that represents how
much a certain gas contributes to global warming in comparison to carbon dioxide (GWP=1 for CO2) [7].
As mentioned in Motivation 1.1, is the refrigerant R-718 or water is in the category of natural refrigerant and
is environmentally friendly. Additionally, it was mentioned in the Objectives 1.2 that the compressor in devel-
opment operates with water as a working ﬂuid. Therefore, understanding why water was chosen as a working
ﬂuid is important.
12
==PAGE:23==
Water oﬀers many advantages as a refrigerant, it is completely nontoxic to the environment, cost eﬃcient and
easily obtained. Water has absolutely no ozone depletion potential nor global warming potential. The refriger-
ant functions with all the typical material in refrigeration systems. Some studies show that water can compete
with other refrigerants and even outperform them at higher temperatures when it comes to the coeﬃcient of
performance [15][16]. However, there are limiting factors of water application. The main ones being a product
of high speciﬁc volume m3/kg at low temperatures compared to traditional working ﬂuids, resulting in high-
pressure ratios and high outlet temperature [15][16]. Additionally, the low atomic weight of water vapor results
in relatively small temperature lift per stage to other heavier chemical ﬂuids [5]. Water is seen as promising
refrigerants for heat pumps for various industrial application over the temperature range of 80-100◦C [7].
In Motivation 1.1 was a short discussion about mechanical vapor recompression (MVR) and it will be continued
here. The MVR technology is applied in an evaporation process, that is the process of removing water from a
product. Essentially, what MVR does, is that it enables a continuous recycling of steam (water vapor) that has
a direct contact with a product for evaporation. Once the steam leaves the evaporation process at a lower energy
level the steam is recycled by recompressing the water vapor to a higher energy level via compressor in a cycle.
This is possible with steam because steam can be in constant with the product and because of thermodynamic
properties of steam that enables high-temperature operations. Energy added to the compressor is the main
driver behind the evaporation process. Commonly, with a conventional technology, the energy content of the
steam experiences a considerably lower eﬀective usage because a large portion of the steam is dissipated. Only
a small portion is dissipated via MVR technology. Recycling the steam demands a considerably lower amount
of energy compared to evaporating liquid water to steam for an evaporation process [17].
Figure 9: Heat ﬂow diagram of an evaporation process with MVR technology [17].
Figure 9 shows a heat ﬂow diagram of an evaporation process with MVR technology. A is the product that
enters the evaporator for evaporation. B is dissipated steam exiting the cycle. C is the product concentrated
leaving the evaporator. E is the electric energy used by the compressor and is the main driver of energy. G is
condensed steam exiting the circle. The compressor is the gray box in the ﬁgure with three rackets in the middle.
The red ﬂow is the discharge stream of water vapor with a higher energy content. As the ﬂow exchanges energy
with the product, the ﬂow cools down and becomes oranges colored. Most parts of this ﬂow are recompressed
except for the dissipated steam B. The orange inlet ﬂow enters the compressor for recompression and the cycle
is repeated [17].
13
==PAGE:24==
1.5.7
Surge and choke
When running a compressor it is critical to know the permissible operating ranges and operate according to
those limitations. Surge and choke are phenomena that jeopardize stable operation. Surge- and chokes lines are
included in the compressor map because of their importance to denote the limit to safe operations [10]. The
purpose of this section is to introduce those phenomena, what are the syndromes and why do they occur.
Whenever a ﬂow is diﬀused along a physical surface and is decelerating, the ﬂow can become disorganized
and its adjacent streamlines are unable to follow the surface. Consequently, the ﬂow around the wall region
will develop a reverse ﬂow along the wall surface. Figure 10 demonstrates this. The ﬂuid momentum in the
streamlines adjacent to the wall cannot overcome the adverse pressure gradient and the viscous shear stresses
along the surface area. A stall occurs when the resistance is strong enough to bring the ﬂuid velocity to a rest
and furthermore reverse ﬂow streamlines along the wall. As a result, the surrounding streamlines are deﬂected
from the surface as the adverse pressure gradient and the viscous shear stresses are locally predominant. A
general syndrome of the stall is noise generation. Stall eﬀects can happen for a several elements of a compressor
without making the entire stage stall [18].
Figure 10: Separation of ﬂow [18].
A stage stall occurs whenever the stall is very robust in one the compressor’s element or a several elements of
the compressor jointly stall. As a consequence, the ﬂow is unstable because the overall pressure ratio versus
ﬂow characteristic is negatively sloped. Meaning that a further ﬂow decrease will result in a decreasing overall
pressure ratio. A general syndrome of stage stall that can occur are random ﬂow and pressure ﬂuctuations [18].
Surge is a phenomenon that brings instability to the operation and can possibly damage or destroy the compres-
sor [10]. In surge, the compressor delivers oscillating ﬂow to the system with a complete reverse of the discharge
ﬂow back to the compressor. The ﬂow is reversed because of the stage stalling. Then the ﬂow is reversed the
pressure on the discharge side will drop. The compressor will rebuild pressure again and the process will repeat
itself repeatedly until the conditions are changed. A compression can be brought to a surge by keeping the
compressor speed ﬁxed and reducing the mass ﬂow until the system can not longer sustain stable operation
[18]. The general syndrome of a surge is increased noise, pulsation of the mass ﬂow and increased mechanical
vibration [10] .
Before introducing the choke phenomenon a brief introduction to the Mach number is needed.
The Mach
number deﬁned as the ratio of a ﬂow velocity to the local speed of sound. For instance, the speed of sound in
a medium is denoted with the number Mach 1. For a perfect gas, the equation of the Mach number can be
written as [10]:
M = c
a =
c
p
γRspecT
(15)
Where M is the Mach number, c is the ﬂuid velocity, a is the sonic velocity of the medium, γ is the ratio of
speciﬁc heats, Rspec is the unique gas constant and T is the ﬂuid temperature. The speciﬁc heat capacity ratio
γ can be written as [13]:
γ = cp
cv
(16)
14
==PAGE:25==
Where γ is the speciﬁc heat capacity ratio, cp is the speciﬁc heat at constant pressure and cv is the speciﬁc heat
at constant volume. The unique gas constant or the speciﬁc gas constant is the universal gas constant divided
by the molar mass of the gas and can be written as [10]:
Rspec = R
M
(17)
Where Rspec is the speciﬁc gas constant, R is the universal gas constant and M molar mass of the gas.
The ﬂow becomes compressible when the Mach number exceeds appropriately the value 0.3 and as a result, the
ﬂuid density can not be considered as a constant. This applies to high power turbomachines hence compressors.
To start with the Mach number is relevant to the choke phenomena in this section. Moreover, the Mach number
is used to determine the stagnation conditions in the pipes. The stagnation conditions will be deﬁned and
explain in methods for stagnation conditions 2.3.3.
Choke is a phenomenon that occurs at supersonic conditions and the mass ﬂow has reached a maximum.
Normally, at subsonic conditions, when the ﬂow velocity and the Mach number are increasing, the mass ﬂow
per unit area also increases. The equation for this relation can be written as [10]:
˙m
A = ρ ∗c
(18)
Where ˙m is the mass ﬂow, A is the cross-sectional area, ρ is the density of the ﬂuid and c is the velocity of
the ﬂow. The mass ﬂow at subsonic condition can increase because the ﬂow velocity c and the Mach number
increase faster than the density ρ of the ﬂuid decreases [10].
For a ﬂow at supersonic conditions and above, the mass ﬂow per unit area is reduced because the ﬂuid density
decreases faster than the ﬂow velocity and the Mach number is increasing. A maximum mass ﬂow is therefore
reached at Mach 1 and the mass ﬂow is choked due to the mass ﬂow limit [10].
When the inlet ﬂow exceeds M ≈0.7, the ﬂow over the blade become transonic and as a result, the performance
deteriorates. Moreover, above sonic speeds (M>1) presence of shock waves lead to additional losses, creating
thicker boundary layers and consequently greater viscous losses due to the rapid pressure rise across the shock
waves [10].
15
==PAGE:26==
1.5.8
Compressor map
Essentially, a compressor map indicates the performance of the compressor at the design conditions and oﬀ-
design conditions. Additionally, shows the compressor map what operating ranges are permissible and safe to
operate on.
The compressor map is made up of four important parameters, those are mass ﬂow, pressure ratio, compressor
speed and the isentropic eﬃciency that is the compressor performance. Figure 11a is an example of a compressor
map and displays those mentioned four parameters. The mass ﬂow appears on the x-axis, the pressure ratio
appears on the y-axis, the compressor speed is illustrated as turbocharger speed lines and the isentropic eﬃciency
makes up the eﬃciency islands. Additionally, to the already mentioned parameters in the ﬁgure, is an illustration
of the surge line and the choke line that show the permissible operating ranges. A compressor map shows in
a visible manner how the key parameters interact with each other. Hence, a compressor map serves as a tool
to choose an appropriate compressor for given project and in order to estimate the compressor’s performance
under various conditions.
(a) Compressor map diagram [19].
(b) A corrected relative compressor map for air from Weel
& Sandvig [20].
Figure 11: Examples of compressor maps
Moreover, a compressor map can be scaled in several ways. A compressor map can be scaled with a corrected
values. Corrected values refer to the conditions if the inlet pressure and temperature would correspond to
standard conditions [21]. An example of this is ﬁgure 11a has a corrected mass ﬂow and compressor speed.
Compressor maps are usually corrected, the corrected values facilitate a comparison between various pressure,
temperature, compressor speed and mass ﬂow conditions. That is because the actual conditions that make up
the compressor map have been brought down to a common platform.
A compressor map can also be relativized, meaning that value of the parameters introduced is a ratio to the
design point values. The design point denotes the mass ﬂow, the pressure ratio compressor speed and the
isentropic eﬃciency that the compressor was designed for. The purpose of making a compressor map being
relatively scaled is the ability to compare various inlet conditions and diﬀerent gases to the design point.
Usually the pressure ratio remains at an actual scale. However, a compressor map can have a reduced relative
pressure ratio scaling [20]. The purpose of a reduced pressure ratio is to scale pressure ratio of the equal inlet
and outlet pressure levels to zero. Thus the reduced pressure ratio scale starts when the pressure ratio exceeds
equal levels over the compressor. The relative part is for design point conditions comparison.
In this case, the scaling of the compressor map is corrected and corrected relativized, respectively. An example
of compressor map that is corrected and relativized can be viewed in ﬁgure 11b. The discussed compressor map
is made by Weel & Sandvig with a computer software, where the air is the working ﬂuid. The pink point seen
on top of an eﬃciency island in the graph is the design point. The corrected relative mass ﬂow forms the x-axis,
the reduced relative pressure ratio forms the y-axis, the corrected relative speed lines go across the eﬃciency
islands like in ﬁgure 11a and the relative eﬃciency makes up the eﬃciency islands. The orange data points are
experimental data points plotted onto the compressor map. This can be done during live compression or by
collecting experimental data and plotting them onto the compressor map like is done in ﬁgure 11a.
16
==PAGE:27==
2
Methodology
This chapter describes the methodology of the experiment. Firstly, by introducing the test rig and its compo-
nents, moreover, explaining its function. This is done to give a basic understanding of the setup. Secondly, the
planning of experiments is introduced. Thirdly, is the analysis and processing of data. This section explains
the process going from raw experimental data to constructing a compressor map.
2.1
The test rig
Brieﬂy about the test rigs assembly. The design work and assembly were carried out by Weel & Sandvig. The
test rig was installed at one of DTU’s test facility, in building 412. Installation started in December 2016 and
the test rig assembly was completed in April 2017. The test rig was however not ready for operation until
May due to issues with the cooling system. Weel & Sandvig in cooperation with the student conducted the
experiments in late May 2017.
Schematics of the test rig is shown in ﬁgures 12 and 13. It is a closed loop system meaning that the ﬂow goes
in circles. Starting from the far left is the compressor. With steam as a working ﬂuid, the compressor usually
operates with a suction line temperature of 80-140◦C with a discharge line temperature from 80 to 250◦C. The
compressor usually compresses from the range 0.8 – 1.1 bar with a compression ratio of approximately 2.5 [22].
The steam entering the compressor must be slightly superheated to prevent damage to the compressor. The
compressor superheats the water vapor even further and then discharges the water vapor through a widening
ﬂange into the discharge line. The discharge line is the lower pipeline in both ﬁgures mentioned above. A
temperature sensor and a pressure sensor are installed on the discharge line. Thereafter, the ﬂow goes through
an oriﬁce plate for mass ﬂow measurement.
The water vapor passes through an automatic throttle valve,
thereafter, if necessary, spray nozzles can inject water vapor to the working to desuperheat it before entering
the condenser. Although it is not shown in the schematics, there is a bypass line installed between the oriﬁce
plate and the automatic throttle valve to the condenser ensuring additional heating to the water vapor and
prevent condensation that can harm the compressor.
The ﬂow through the bypass line is controlled by a
manual valve. The condenser, shown on the far right, cools down the water vapor. However, the water vapor
should exit the heat exchanger to the suction line slightly superheated to prevent droplet formation.
Figure 12: Side view schematic of the test rig by Weel & Sandvig.
17
==PAGE:28==
Figure 13: Above view schematic of the test rig by Weel & Sandvig.
The condenser and the spray nozzles (the cooling system) uses external water for cooling. A plate heat ex-
changer is connected to the condenser. The ﬂow of the cooling water to the cold side of the plate heat exchanger
is regulated by a valve. This controls the pool water temperature thereby aﬀecting the suction pressure and
temperature.
On the suction line (the upper line) is a manual expansion valve. Additionally, there is a temperature sensor
and a pressure sensor installed on the suction line. Finally, a sight glass is installed to enable visual observation
of the ﬂow. Moreover, the pipelines in the cycle are coated with an insulation to reduces heat losses.
Figure 14 shows the test rig assembled and in operation. A box was later placed around the compressor to
reduce the sound pollution. Other than that the ﬁgure represents the schematic relevant for the project.
Figure 14: The test rig assembled and in operation by author.

==PAGE:29==
2.1.1
The compressor
As mentioned in the Motivation 1.1, the compressor design was a response to increased interests for industrial
waste heat recovery, a shifting landscape of refrigerants and as well to reduce the fossil fuel consumption. This
section aims to elaborate on the compressor’s speciﬁcation and possibility of application.
Brieﬂy about the speciﬁcation, the compressor is centrifugal and runs with water vapor (steam). The compressor
is being developed to operate as an industrial heat pump for the temperature range of 90-110 ◦C. The heat
pump capacity is in the range of 100-500 kilowatts (kW) with a temperature lift of 20-25 ◦C per stage. The
compressor can increase the temperature more than 20-25 ◦C, however, the meaning of a temperature lift is
associated with the saturation temperature at inlet and outlet. The largest part of the energy is transferred at
saturation temperature when the ﬂuid is undergoing a phase shift. The compressor’s design oﬀers possibilities
of various application, it can be installed in series and parallel channels. For example, a two-stage conﬁguration
can deliver temperature lifts between 25-50 ◦C and for a three-stage conﬁguration, the temperature lift goes up
to about 75 ◦C [5]. Model of the compressor is shown in ﬁgure 15.
Figure 15: The compressor prototype made with a computer aided design [23].
The compressor is developed from Rotrex suit of automobile turbochargers with a housing made of aluminum
and an impeller made of titanium. By basing the compressor design on a high-speed gear used in automotive
turbochargers, it allows the usage of a standard electric motor drive line instead of commonly used expensive
high-speed motors with a direct drive. The compressor has an outstanding traction gear with a step-up ratio
of 7.5 and transmission eﬃciency of 98.5% at full load. The low-speed shaft runs at 12,000 rounds per minute
(rpm) at full load delivering an impeller speed up to 90,000 rpm. An internal oil pump lubricates the traction
gear and circulates the oil through the external oil cooler. The compressor itself has a capacity of approximately
50 kW [5]. Figure 16 displays a cross-sectional view of the compressor and the gear assembly.
Figure 16: The gear equipment in the compressor made with a computer aided design [24].
19
==PAGE:30==
2.1.2
The control system
A software from Weel & Sandvig (WS Turbo) is used to control the speed of the compressor, the superheat in
the suction line and the throttle valve position. This software has a compressor shutdown schedule if certain
parameters exceed a certain limit e.g. in the case of insuﬃcient cooling supply to the system or a compressor
overheating. Additionally, if the compressor receives insuﬃcient oil ﬂow for a certain period then the system
initiates a compressor shutdown schedule. The control system has anti-surge protection strategy for the com-
pressor. If the compressor violates speciﬁed minimum surge margin the control system gradually opens the
discharge valve. In the case of a fully open discharge valve and choke violation, the compressor goes into a shut-
down phase. Moreover, the compressor has a restraint on maximum allowable delivery power and the discharge
pressure. Power supply to the electric motor will be cut oﬀif the winding temperature exceeds allowed limit.
The control system modulates the cooling water ﬂow to ensure suﬃcient superheating [25].
Figure 17: Control system diagram by Weel & Sandvig.
20
==PAGE:31==
2.1.3
Instruments
The instrument mentioned in this section are temperature sensors, pressure sensors, and a diﬀerential pressure
sensor. The instrumental sensors make up the most experimental data needed to build a compressor map.
There are pressure and temperature sensors on the suction line and the discharge line. Diﬀerential pressure
sensor is an instrument that measures the pressure drop over the oriﬁce plate that lays the foundation to mass
ﬂow calculations.
Beginning with the temperature sensors, both of them that are placed on the suction line and the discharge
come from Jensen Electric, are type Pt100/B [26]. The temperature sensors are a thermocouple sensor with a
3 wire connection. The temperature sensors transmit current in the range of 4-20mA. The temperature range
is 0-300 ◦C according to the product speciﬁcation [26]. Surprisingly, the accuracy is not listed in the product
speciﬁcation. Luckily, it is this the same type of a temperature sensor as used in a previous experiment by Weel
& Sandvig. The company’s report states that the accuracy of the temperature sensor corresponds to ± 0.75 ◦C
[20]. The temperature sensor can be viewed in ﬁgure 18.
Figure 18: Pt100/B thermocouple temperature sensor from Jensens Electric [26].
Continuing with the pressure sensor placed on the suction line. It comes from Danfoss and the type is AKS 33
060G2047 [27]. The pressure sensor converts the measured value into a 4-20 mA output signal. The pressure
range is from -1 to 5 bar with a typical accuracy of 0.3 % of full scale, corresponding to ± 0.018 bar. The
pressure sensor can be viewed in ﬁgure 19.
Figure 19: AKS 33 060G2047 pressure transmitter from Danfoss [27].
The pressure sensor placed on the discharge line comes from Huba Control and the type is 511.015 [28]. The
pressure sensor converts the measured value into a 4-20 mA output signal. The pressure range is from 0 to 4
bar with a max accuracy of 0.3 % of full scale, corresponding to ± 0.012 bar. The pressure sensor can be viewed
in ﬁgure 20.
21
==PAGE:32==
Figure 20: Pressure sensor from Huba Control, type 511.015 [28].
The pressure drop sensor or the diﬀerential pressure sensor is placed on the suction close to the oriﬁce plate.
The diﬀerential pressure is comes from Huba Control, type 692.906007121 [29]. The diﬀerential pressure sensor
converts the measured value into a 4-20 mA output signal. The pressure range is from 0 to 0.5 bar with a max
accuracy of 0.4 % of full scale, corresponding to ± 0.002 bar. The pressure sensor can be viewed in ﬁgure 21.
Figure 21: Diﬀerential pressure sensor type 692 from Huba Control [29].
2.1.4
The cooling system
The plate heat exchanger will always be provided with a minimum ﬂow of cooling water thus having a minimum
open position of the ball valve. The cooling load will mostly correspond to the electrical power consumption
of the system, some heat is removing from the system via heating loss. A circulation pump circulates the
warm ﬂuid from the condenser back to the condenser and some for cooling injection. The circulation pump is
manufactured by Pedrollo, type PQ60.
2.1.5
Environmental risk
Under normal condition, no water, steam or other gases are released to the surroundings. However, if the
pressure in the system exceeds security level making the pressure safety valve open and releasing steam into
the test facility. The system contains approximately 40 kg of water thereof are 0.14 kg or 0.08 m3 steam.
Additionally, to water, traction oil is presenting in the system to control the compressor temperature. The
quantity is approximately 1 kg [25]. The compressor has a surrounding frame to reduce the sounds pollution
below DTU’s standard. Additionally, an installation of a hard plastic plate to shield bodies in the case of a
mechanical failure was made.
2.1.6
System supply
A form of external supply to the system is cooling water and electrical power. The electrical power that the
compressor receives is restrained by the supply capacity of around 42.5 kW. The power is controlled via Parker
Ac 30 series variable speed drive. The cooling load mostly corresponds to the electrical power consumption of
the system. The water boiler is VIda XL and can contain 80 liters of hot water and has a maximum heating
temperature up to 75◦C.
22
==PAGE:33==
2.1.7
Data collection
The sensors in the system send an analog output signal to ADAM 5000L/TCP data sampling system. The
sampling system is connected to a Linksys router that serves as a data bridge between the data system to
transfer real-time data to the operating computer and the data collection viewer respectively.
All measurement data during the experiment are put into an Excel ﬁle and further analyzed to obtain a data
selection.
The data contains information about the timing of each measurement, the suction line pressure
and temperature, the discharge line pressure and temperature, the pressure drop over the oriﬁce plate, the
motor speed and the impeller speed. The data used to estimate the compressor’s performance and to build the
compressor map is exported from the data collection viewer.
2.1.8
Start up conditions
When starting operations for cold condition some measures must be taken due to water pools in the cycle. In
the suction and discharge line, the water is drained out by manual valves in the drain pipes. Thereafter, the
remaining water is dried out by running the compressor at low speed and meanwhile heating up the system by
blowing superheated steam throughout the test rig. Once, the system is hot enough, the compressor speed can
be increased slowly with constant speed stops making sure the system can balance.
2.2
Planning of experiments
The initial plan was to run the compressor for 31,500 rpm, 40,500 rpm, 49,500 rpm, 58,500 rpm, 63,000 rpm,
72,000 rpm, 81,000 rpm and 90,000 rpm. In total, eight diﬀerent speeds that cover the main operational area.
Due to higher sensibility in the system and increasing error eﬀects, speeds under 31,500 rpm were not included
in the planning. Furthermore, it is very unlikely that the compressor will operate in practice at lower speeds
than 31,500 rpm because the temperature lift is minor.
The procedure, meanwhile keeping each speed constant, is to balance the system initially with a fully open value
and from there close the value in periodically until reaching a surge condition. The system needs re-balancing
every time the value position is alternated. It was assumed that changed the value by 5 degrees in each step
until surge can represent the various conditions under a constant speed curve. Once completed, the compressor
speed is to change to the next desired value and the method is repeated.
23
==PAGE:34==
2.3
Analysis of data
This section deals with the whole data processing in order to create the compressor maps according to the
objectives. Essentially, the data processing are divided into few categories. The ﬁrst is the data selection and
import 2.3.1, the second is the ﬂow calculation 2.3.2, the third being the stagnation conditions 2.3.3, the fourth
is the heat loss 2.3.4, the ﬁfth is the compressor performance 2.3.5, the sixth is the compressor map 2.3.7 and
the seventh is the uncertainty 2.3.6.
The experimental data were analyzed in Excel, once proper data were derived then they were moved to Matlab
for calculations. The main Matlab script is compressormap.m and executes the main calculations. However,
there are additional scripts that contain the thermodynamical functions of entropy, enthalpy and so on with
an add-on program named FluidProp. FluidProp, made by Asymptote comes with a large variety of ﬂuids and
ﬂuid mixtures, such as thermodynamic properties of water and steam according to International Association
for the Properties of Water and Steam (IAPWS). Main codes, analyzed experimental data and results can be
found in the appendix 5.
2.3.1
Data selection and import
The data collection is large and this section explains how the data were sorted out and prepared for further
processing. All measurement data stored by the data collection viewer are exported to an Excel ﬁle for further
processing. The data contains information about the timing of each measurement, the suction line pressure and
temperature, the discharge line pressure and temperature and the pressure drop over the oriﬁce plate.
Essentially, the main goal here with data selection was to sort stable data from the unstable. Unstable data do
not give the correct information of the compressor’s performance. In order to locate stable data trajectories, the
data are plotted and allegedly stable intervals are chosen from the plots for further processing. Furthermore, to
see whether the allegedly stable data are stable enough they need to be processed with statistics.
Before explaining the statistics behind the data processing it is necessary to state that the cooling system was
dysfunctional and had to be operated manually. As a result making the system balancing a great challenge. To
elaborate further on that, in an open looped system the ﬂow in suction line enters the compressor and is then
discharged from the compressor, however, the ﬂow does not enter the system again. In other words, discharge
line conditions are results of the suction line conditions and the compressor work. The discharged ﬂow does not
aﬀect conditions in the suction line. Therefore when changing, for instance, the compressor speed, the system
will reach a new balance once it has overcome the thermal inertia, given that the conditions will remain the
same. On the other hand, when making a change to a closed loop system, the ﬂow goes in circles. As a result,
the system will need to balance the discharged ﬂow before reentering into the compressor. Consequently, if the
discharged ﬂow is not balanced before entering the suction line again, the suction line ﬂow can magnitude the
eﬀects at the compressor outlet.
When selection the allegedly stable intervals for selection, the thermal inertia of the system has to be taken
into account. The data show that changes in pressure level are more rapid and frequent compared to the tem-
peratures. Then by changing the compressor speed, the mass ﬂow or the cooling load, the pressure responses
faster to it. The thermal inertia of the system is higher and takes a long time to balance compared to pressure
inertia. Ideally, the intervals would be long and stable enough for the system to overcome the thermal inertia.
The system was hard to balance and rarely were the parameters balancing in a satisfactory manner, especially
all simultaneously. Rather that the parameters’ rate of change trending upwards or downwards, was slowed
down in many cases. At higher compressor speeds manual control of the cooling system was more manageable.
Additionally, the intervals in question vary in length but are most under ﬁve minutes. To minimize the eﬀects
of thermal inertia and unresting of the temperature and pressure parameters, the focus was on ﬁnding intervals
that ﬂuctuate remotely for at least a period of one minute, and with temperature in a descending order or has
more than one local temperature maximum over the interval.
Now back to the statistics. Firstly, a sample mean or the average of the interval is found. An average of intervals
is taken instead of selecting a single value because one measurement might deviate from other measurements in
the interval, however, by taking an average from many measurements the deviations are more likely to even out.
24
==PAGE:35==
This increases the conﬁdence in the measurement data. A sample mean is the sum of the values in a chosen
interval and then dividing the sum by the number of samples. This average value will be processed further
to construct the compressor map if the interval was stable enough. The equation for the sample mean or the
average can be written as [30]:
µ = ¯X = 1
n ∗
n
X
i=1
Xi
(19)
Where ¯X is the mean, n is the number of samples and Xi is the sample value. A standard deviation is a tool
to measure the degree of uncertainty in a sample. A low standard deviation indicates that the data points are
close to the mean value and likewise a high standard deviation indicates the sample is widespread. Hence, in
this case indicating whether is the data interval is stable or not. The equation for a standard deviation can be
written as [30]:
σ = s =
v
u
u

1
n −1 ∗

n
X
i=1
X2


(20)
Where s is the standard deviation. In order to determine the magnitude of the standard deviation, the coeﬃcient
of variation, also known as the relative standard deviation is applied. In other words, the coeﬃcient of variation
shows how large the uncertainty is proportional to the average value taken. The equation for the coeﬃcient of
variation can be written as [31]:
Cv = σ
µ
(21)
Where Cv is the coeﬃcient of variation, σ is the standard deviation and µ is the sample mean.
The data intervals used for further processing are of various time lengths, they can be up to ten minutes but
never less than one minute. Some of them were showing a higher standard deviation and coeﬃcient of variation
than preferable. It was decided to make a limit on the coeﬃcient of variation for all of the data intervals of
1,25% for the temperatures and pressure levels. This limit reduced the uncertainty considerably and convinced
the author that the data interval was stable enough by the limit. The data intervals were trimmed from the
ends, sometimes only in one end, sometimes at both ends, depending on where the data showed a higher degree
of deviation than allowed by the set limit. A smaller limit by the coeﬃcient of variation would have meant that
the uncertainty should be lower, however, that would have to result in even shorter data intervals. Thereby
reducing the conﬁdence in the average value. Hence, the value of 1,25% for the coeﬃcient of variant is a com-
promise between the length of the intervals and the magnitude of error.
The data points imported are systematically lined up at various compressor speed intervals. However, within
each constant compressor speed level, the data points are at various pressure ratios and mass ﬂows. An internal
structure of those various pressure ratios and mass ﬂows was implemented, the order ranged from the largest
mass ﬂow (choke line) to the smallest mass ﬂow (surge line). The internal and outer structure facilitates further
processing in Matlab and reduces the odds of errors in further data processing, that is the main drive behind
the structure. All of the usable data collected and lined up systematically were then imported into Matlab for
further processing via xlsread.
Due to a calibration error, the pressure sensor on the suction line was underestimating the absolute pressure by
0.2 bar. The correct measurement value was updated by adding 0.2 onto the old values in excel. Additionally,
due to a calibration error, there is a proportional measurement error by the diﬀerential pressure sensor. Figure
22 shows the measurement error calculated by Weel & Sandvig.
25
==PAGE:36==
Figure 22: Proportional calibration error of instruments.
The blue line is the calculated pressure drop, the red line is the measurement by the diﬀerential pressure sensor
on day 1 and ﬁnally, the green line is the measurement by the diﬀerential pressure sensor on day 2.
The
calculated pressure drop is found by the height diﬀerence in the pressure tubes. The pressure tubes usually
enter the diﬀerential pressure sensor. By placing those tubes into the air ﬁlled with water, the pressure diﬀerence
will create a height diﬀerence in the water level. The formula to convert the height diﬀerence to pressure can
be written as [32]:
∆P = ρ ∗x ∗g
103 ∗105
(22)
Where ∆P is the diﬀerential pressure, ρ is the density of water at 22◦C, x is the height diﬀerence and g is the
gravity constant. The constants in the denominator are correctional values, they convert the input from cm to
m and the output from Pa to bar, respectively.
Figure 23: Calibration error as a function of pressure drop.
26
==PAGE:37==
There are two proportional error curves in ﬁgure 22, one on each day, to compromise an average proportional
error is taken and used to make up for the calibration error. Figure 23 shows the average calibration error as a
function of pressure drop measured by the diﬀerential pressure sensor. The error trendline seen in the ﬁgure is
a ﬁfth-degree polynomial. The trendline equation can be written as:
δ∆P = 13, 195(∆P)5 −3, 958.4(∆P)4 + 441.86(∆P)3 −22.325(∆P)2 + 0.5634(∆P) −0.0004
(23)
Where δ∆P is the error as a function of pressure drop ∆P. The ﬁfth-degree polynomial had the best trend along
the error curve. Based on the measurement value by the diﬀerential pressure sensor, the trendline gives the
error value that is then added to the measured value. Thereby the pressure drop is corrected before calculating
the mass ﬂow rate.
The initial pressure ratio is given in the data selection results 3.1. The pressure ratio is the ratio of pressure over
the compressor, in other words, the ratio of the discharge line pressure to the suction line pressure. Pressure
ratio is a key parameter in the compressor map. The equation of a pressure ratio can be written as [13]:
Pr = PD
PS
(24)
Where Pr is the pressure ratio, PD is the discharge line pressure and PS is the suction line pressure. The
pressure ratio is updated later on according to the stagnation pressure levels, however, the formula is the same
with or without stagnation conditions.

==PAGE:38==
2.3.2
Flow calculation
Flow calculations are of interest because the mass ﬂow rate is a key parameter in the compressor map. More-
over, by ﬁnding the mass ﬂow rate, the ﬂuid velocity can be found and hence the stagnation temperature and
pressure in the system.
In order to know the mass ﬂow rate, a thin plate, called the oriﬁce plate was placed in the discharge pipe. The
volumetric or mass ﬂow rate calculation is made by means of the oriﬁce plate. This is done by measuring the
pressure diﬀerence across the oriﬁce plate by knowing the characteristics of the ﬂuid and the circumstances of
the device. In this section, ﬁrst some key parameters in the mass ﬂow rate formula are introduced, then the
mass ﬂow formula itself, from the mass ﬂow rate, the volumetric ﬂow on both sides is obtained and ﬁnally, the
ﬂuid speed on both sides of the compressor.
The discharge coeﬃcient 26 relates actual ﬂow to the theoretical ﬂowrate. It is a dimensionless factor. The
discharge coeﬃcient can be viewed as a correction factor when calculating the mass ﬂow. The Stolz equation
for the discharge coeﬃcient is [20]:
C = 0.5959+0.0312∗β2.1−0.1840∗β8+0.0029∗β2.5∗
 106
ReD
0.75
+0.0900∗L1∗β4∗(1−β4)−1−0.0337∗L2∗β3 (25)
Where β is the diameter ratio of the oriﬁce diameter d to the pipe diameter D. ReD is the Reynolds number in
the upstream pipe, L1 and L2 are the relative pressure tapping spacing L = l/D. Where is l is the distance of
pressure tapings to the oriﬁce plate and D is the pipe diameter. The initial Reynolds number is a guess value.
The Reynolds number is a dimensionless parameter and the equation for ﬂow in a pipe can be written as [32]:
ReD = ρ1V d
µ
(26)
Where ReD is the Reynolds number, ρ is the ﬂuid density, V is the ﬂuid velocity, d is the pipe inner diameter
and µ is the dynamic viscosity. Next is the expansibility factor, that takes into account the compressibility of
the ﬂuid [33]:
ϵ = 1 −(0.41 + 0.35 ∗β4) ∗
∆p
κ ∗p1
(27)
Where β is the same as before, ∆p is the diﬀerential pressure over the oriﬁce plate, κ is the isentropic exponent
of the gas and p1 is the upstream pressure. Finally, the equation for mass ﬂow can be written as [34]:
qm = C ∗ϵ ∗π
4 ∗d2 ∗
√2 ∗∆p ∗ρ1
p
1 −β4
(28)
Where qm is the mass ﬂow rate. C is the discharge coeﬃcient, β is the diameter ratio of the oriﬁce diameter
d to the pipe diameter D. ϵ is the expandability factor, ρ1 is the ﬂuid density in the upstream and ∆p is the
pressure diﬀerence across the oriﬁce plate. Then the volumetric ﬂow qv is derived from dividing the upstream
ﬂuid density ρ1 into the massﬂow qm [34]:
qv = qm
ρ
(29)
The ﬂuid velocity has to be derived to carry out the measurement data corrections. The equation for velocity
is [32]:
V = qv
A
(30)
Where V is the velocity, qv is the volumetric ﬂow and A is the pipe’s cross-sectional area. An iteration is applied
to the ﬂow calculation with an updated velocity, hence deriving a more accurate Reynolds number. Later, once
the stagnation conditions have been derived the ﬂow calculations are re-run based on new thermal properties.

==PAGE:39==
2.3.3
Stagnation Conditions
The temperature and pressure sensors do not measure the total temperature and pressure, respectively. Thus,
the measurement data has to be corrected by calculation to stagnation (total) conditions to derive the truth
performance of the compressor. The pressure sensor read the static pressure and the temperature sensor reads
the static temperature and partially the dynamic temperature [20].
Stagnation pressure (total pressure) equals to the static pressure when the ﬂow is brought to rest isentropically.
The ratio of stagnation pressure to static pressure for a compressible ﬂow can be written as [10]:
p0
p =

1 + γ −1
2
M 2

γ
γ−1
(31)
Where P0 is the stagnation pressure, P is the static pressure, γ is the speciﬁc heat capacity ratio and M
is the Mach number. The equations for γ and M are found in the section for surge and choke 1.5.7. The
dynamic pressure is a factor in determining the isentropic eﬃciency uncertainty and is therefore found. The
dynamic pressure is the diﬀerence between the stagnation pressure and the static pressure and the relation for
a compressible ﬂow can be written as [10]:
pd = p0 −p
(32)
Where Pd is the dynamic pressure, P0 is the stagnation pressure and P is the static pressure.
Stagnation
temperature (total temperature) equals to the static temperature when the ﬂow is brought to rest isentropically.
The stagnation temperature is related to the stagnation/static pressure ratio. Assuming no heat or work transfer
done to the ﬂuid along with isentropic conditions, the relation for a compressible ﬂow can be written as [10]:
p0
p =
To
T

γ
γ−1
(33)
Where To is the stagnation temperature and T is the static temperature. The dynamic temperature is a factor
in determining the isentropic eﬃciency uncertainty and is therefore found. The dynamic temperature is the
diﬀerence between the stagnation pressure and the static pressure and the relation for a compressible ﬂow can
be written as [10]:
Td = To −T
(34)
Where Td is the dynamic temperature, To is the stagnation temperature and T is the static temperature. The
recovery factor (R.F.) stands for the proportion of dynamic temperature measured by the temperature sensor.
The recovery factor value 0.7. The equation for the recovery factor can be written as [35]:
R.F. = Tm −T
To −T
(35)
Where R.F. is the recovery factor, Tm is the measured temperature, T is the static temperature and To is the
stagnation temperature. Subsequently, in order to derive the correct static temperature and thus the stagnation
temperature and the dynamics temperature, an iteration via while loop in Matlab was applied. The constraint
can be written as:
Tm = T + R.F. ∗Td
(36)
Where Tm is the measured temperature, T is the static temperature, R.F. is the recovery factor and Td is the
dynamic temperature. During the iteration, a small value was withdrawn per run in the loop from the static
temperature because the initial static temperature value derived from the measurement value was initially over-
shooting the correct static temperature value. This is done until the constraint is satisﬁed.
Once the stagnation conditions have been found then the ﬂow calculation can be iterated again. Additionally,
the performance of the compressor can be calculated.
29
==PAGE:40==
2.3.4
Heat loss
There is a slight reduction in the working ﬂuid temperature between the temperature sensors and the compressor.
Heat losses aﬀect the compressor performance and are therefore necessary to account for. The ﬂow lines (suction
and discharge lines) are mostly thermally insulated and are assumed to be adiabatic except for the sightseeing
glass on the suction line. The sightseeing glass is assumed to be diabatic and responsible for the heat loss. The
Figure 24 shows the sightseeing glass.
Figure 24: The sightseeing glass by author.
Firstly, to obtain the how the sightseeing glass and its medium resist to a heat ﬂow, the thermal resistance is
found. The principal equation for thermal resistance of a pipe can be written as [36]:
X
R′′
t = R′′
tot =
1
h1 ∗2 ∗π ∗r1 ∗L +
ln(r2/r1)
2 ∗π ∗k ∗L +
1
h2 ∗2 ∗π ∗r2 ∗L
(37)
Where Rtot is the thermal resistance of the ﬂuids and the pipe material along with the surface area. The variable
h1 is the heat transfer coeﬃcient of steam. The variable r1 is the inner radius of the pipe, from the center of
the pipe until the pipe wall. The variable L is the length of the pipe. The variable r2 is the outer radius of the
pipe, from the center of the pipe through the pipe wall thickness. The variable h2 is the heat transfer coeﬃcient
of air. The overall heat transfer coeﬃcient is an inverse of the thermal resistants and is a measurement of how
well heat is transferred with regards to the temperature diﬀerence in this case. The equation for the overall
heat transfer coeﬃcient U can be written as [36]:
U =
1
R′′
tot
(38)
Where U is the overall heat transfer coeﬃcient. Finally, the equation for the heat transfer can be written as
[36]:
˙Qhl = U ∗∆T
1, 000
(39)
Where
˙Qhl is the heat transfer and ∆T is the temperature diﬀerence of the steam to the surrounding air
temperature. Once the heat transfer has been found, an estimation of the speciﬁc enthalpy diﬀerence can be
carried out by dividing the mass ﬂow with the heat transfer. The equation for the diﬀerence in enthalpy can
be written as [36]:
∆h =
˙Qhl
˙m = cP ∗(Tm,o −Tm,i)
(40)
Where h is the speciﬁc enthalpy diﬀerence due to heat losses. The variable ˙Qhl is the heat transfer. The
variable cP is the speciﬁc heat capacity of the steam. Tm,o is the temperature out of sightseeing glass. Tm,i is
the temperature into sightseeing glass.
A new speciﬁc enthalpy is found by withdrawing the speciﬁc enthalpy diﬀerence from the previous condition.
Pressure is assumed to be constant and by knowing those thermodynamic properties, a newly updated temper-
ature of the working ﬂuid is found.
