==PAGE:41==
2.3.5
Compressor’s performance
An introduction to the isentropic eﬃciency of a compressor has already been given in Introduction 1.5.3.
Isentropic eﬃciency is one of the key parameters in a compressor map. This section is partly to give a reminder
how the isentropic eﬃciency of a compressor is derived. Once the stagnation conditions and the heat loss have
been found the isentropic eﬃciency can be calculated. The isentropic eﬃciency of a compressor can be written
as [13]:
ηisen = Isentropic compressor work
Actual compressor work
= h2,s −h1
h2 −h1
(41)
Where ηisen is the isentropic eﬃciency, h2,s is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the outlet for the isentropic process, h2
is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the outlet for the actual process and h1 is the speciﬁc enthalpy at the inlet.
When evaluating a compressor’s performance there can be several other factors of interest such as global eﬃ-
ciency, inverter eﬃciency, motor eﬃciency, gearbox eﬃciency along with isentropic eﬃciency. Global eﬃciency
indicates how eﬀectively the electric power supplied to the system is being delivered to the working ﬂuid [20].
Inverter eﬃciency indicates how eﬀectively the inverter converts a direct current (DC) to alternating current
(AC) [37]. Motor eﬃciency indicates how eﬀectively the motor converts electrical power input to mechanical
power output [38]. Gear eﬃciency indicates how eﬀectively the gearbox transmits the input power [39].
To make up the compressor map as described in the objectives of the project 1.2 the focus is on the isentropic
eﬃciency, as a result, other kinds of eﬃciencies are excluded from the results.
31
==PAGE:42==
2.3.6
Uncertainty
Measurements derived from experiments always leave some uncertainty. The value found is not necessarily the
truth value. However, the true value is most likely found within the error range.
The isentropic eﬃciency and the mass ﬂow uncertainty are the uncertainties most of the interest. It makes
sense to run the compressor is the most eﬃcient manner, therefore ﬁnding the isentropic eﬃciency uncertainty
is of interest because it shows the error range that the true value is most likely to be found in. The mass ﬂow
uncertainty is of interest because it makes up the compressor map and hence the accuracy of it.
The method of estimating the mass ﬂow uncertainty is in accordance to ISO-5167-1:2003. The equation for the
mass ﬂow uncertainty combines uncertainties with diﬀerent units via fractional uncertainty method [34]. The
equation 42 for the uncertainty can be written as [34]:
δqm
qm
=
sδC
C
2
+
δϵ
ϵ
2
+
 2β4
1 −β4
2
∗
δD
D
2
+

2
1 −β4
2
∗
δd
d
2
+ 1
4
δ∆p
∆p
2
+ 1
4
δρ
ρ
2
(42)
Where δqm/qm is the fractional uncertainty of the mass ﬂow rate. δC/C is the fractional uncertainty of the
discharge coeﬃcient. δϵ/ϵ is the fractional uncertainty of the expansibility factor. β is the diameter ratio of
the oriﬁce diameter to the pipe diameter. δD/D is the fractional uncertainty of the pipe diameter. δd/d is the
fractional uncertainty of the oriﬁce plate diameter. δ∆p/∆p is the fractional uncertainty of the pressure drop
over the oriﬁce plate. δρ/ρ is the fractional uncertainty of the ﬂuid density.
Some of the variables mentioned are dependent on other variables in the formula. However, for practical pur-
poses to is suﬃcient to assume that they are independent of each other. The ﬂow coeﬃcient C is a function
of d, D, ρ and another. The expansibility factor ϵ is a function of d, D, ∆p and another [34]. The fractional
uncertainty for the discharge coeﬃcient, the expansibility coeﬃcient and both of the diameters are derived by
another steam compressor rig test by Weel & Sandvig [20]. The fractional uncertainty of density and pressure
drop are both calculated by the fractional uncertainty method. The uncertainty of density is a function of
temperature and pressure uncertainties and the uncertainties of pressure drop is proportional and the error is
given by the producer.
The uncertainty of isentropic eﬃciency uses the sensitivity method. The independent variables the isentropic
eﬃciency uncertainty are temperatures and pressure levels over the compressor. The temperature error consists
of an instrument error, a dynamic temperature error and a heat loss error. The equation can be written as [30]:
± δT = ±δTins + ±δTdyn + ±δThl
(43)
Where δT is the accumulated temperature error, δTins is the instrument measurement error given by the
producer, δTdyn is the dynamic temperature error and was assumed to be 20% of the dynamic temperature
calculated. Finally, δThl is the temperature drops caused by heat losses from the instruments to the compressor’s
inlet. The temperature drop error was assumed to be 20%. The pressure error consists of an instrument error
and a dynamic pressure error. Pressure losses from the instruments to the compressor’s inlet are considered to
be negligible. The equation can be written as [30]:
± δP = ±δPins + ±δPdyn
(44)
Where δP is the accumulated pressure error, δPins is the instrument measurement error given by the producer,
δPdyn is the dynamic pressure error and was assumed to be 20% of the total pressure temperature calculated.
The sensitivity to each independent variable is then found by adding the error onto the variable while keeping
other independent variables constant and estimating the diﬀerence in isentropic eﬃciency. This is then repeated
for all independent parameters. The equations for sensitivity of each parameter can be written as [20]:
δηT 1 = ηuncer(T1 ± δT1, T2, P1, P2) −η
δηT 2 = ηuncer(T1, T2 ± δT2, P1, P2) −η
δηP 1 = ηuncer(T1, T2, P1 ± δP1, P2) −η
δηP 2 = ηuncer(T1, T2, P1, P2 ± δP2) −η
(45)
Where δηT 1, δηT 2, δηP 1 and δηP 2 is the change in the isentropic eﬃciency by each speciﬁed parameter in the
subscript. T1 is the inlet temperature, T2 is the outlet temperature, P1 is the inlet pressure and P2 is the
32
==PAGE:43==
outlet pressure. ±δT1, ±δT2, ±δP1 and ±δP2 is the uncertainty of temperature and pressure, respectively.
The isentropic eﬃciency function ηuncer(T1, T2, P1, P2) ﬁnds the new isentropic eﬃciency by adding the error
to the input value of each parameter at the time. η is the original value of the isentropic eﬃciency. When this
has been repeated for all parameters it is time to combine the uncertainties. This can be written as [30]:
δηtot =
q
δη2
T 1 + δη2
T 2 + δη2
P 1 + δη2
P 2
(46)
Where δηtot is the total isentropic eﬃciency uncertainty, δηT 1 represents uncertainty by caused T1, δηT 2 repre-
sents uncertainty by caused T2, δηP 1 represents uncertainty by caused P1 and ﬁnally, δηP 2 represents uncertainty
caused by P2.

==PAGE:44==
2.3.7
Compressor map
Finally, at this point, the methodology to derive the actual mass ﬂow, the actual pressure ratio and the actual
isentropic eﬃciency of the compressor have been explained. However, in order to make the corrected compressor
map and the corrected relative compressor map, the values of mass ﬂows, compressor speeds, pressure ratios
and isentropic eﬃciency has to alternated from actual conditions to ﬁt the compressor map criteria as explained
in the introduction.
Beginning with the corrected conditions, the corrected mass ﬂow is the mass ﬂow that would occur at standard
condition and the corrected compressor speed corresponds to the compressor speed at standard conditions [21].
The equation for a compressible mass ﬂow corresponding to a standard conditions can be written as [20]:
˙mcor = ˙m ∗
p
Z ∗R ∗T/Zs ∗Rs ∗Ts
P/Ps
(47)
Where ˙mcor is the corrected mass ﬂow, ˙m is the measured mass ﬂow, Z is the compressibility factor, R is the
gas constant unique to the substance, that is the gas constant divided by the molar mass [40], T is the inlet
stagnation temperature in Kelvin and P is the inlet stagnation pressure. Zs is the compressibility factor at
standard conditions, Rs is the unique gas constant at standard conditions, Ts is the temperature at a standard
condition and Ps is is the pressure level at standard condition.
The compressibility factor corrects for the
compressibility of the steam at the actual conditions. The ratio of unique gas constant corrects for the diﬀerent
gases. This enables correction for diﬀerent types of gases being brought down to a common basis at standard
condition. In this case, air is the unique gas constant at standard conditions. The compressor was initially
designed for air and this procedure facilitates comparison of air and steam mediums.
The equation for a
corrected speed corresponding to standard conditions can be written as [21]:
Ncor = N/
p
Z ∗R ∗T/Zs ∗Rs ∗Ts
(48)
Where Ncor is the corrected speed of the compressor, N is the actual speed, Z is the compressibility factor,
R is the actual unique gas constant and T is the stagnation temperature. Zs is the compressibility factor at
standard conditions, Rs is the unique gas constant of at standard conditions and Ts is the temperature at
standard condition.
Mainly, once the gas experience a lower temperature, a higher pressure or comes close to a phase change more
that gas draws apart from the ideal behavior. Hence, by applying the compressibility factor, real gas behavior
can be accounted for. The compressibility factor equation can be written as [40]:
Z =
P
ρ ∗R ∗T
(49)
Where Z is the compressibility factor, P is the pressure, ρ is the density of the gas, T is the temperature in Kelvin
and ﬁnally, R is the gas constant unique to the substance, that is the gas constant divided by the molar mass [40].
The pressure ratio is the ratio of pressure over the compressor as introduced in the Data selection and import
section 2.3.1. Now, the pressure ratio is derived from the stagnation pressure across the compressor but the
formula remains the same. The equation of a pressure ratio can be written as [13]:
Pr,t = PD,t
PS,t
(50)
Where Pr,t is the stagnation pressure ratio, PD,t is the stagnation outlet pressure and PS,t is stagnation inlet
pressure. The relative part refers to the ratio of the current value to the reference value or the design. The
equation for the relative corrected mass ﬂow that forms the x-axis of the compressor map can be written as
[20]:
˙mc,r =
˙mcor
˙mc,ref
(51)
Where ˙mc,r is the non-dimension relative compressor inlet mass ﬂow, ˙mcor is the corrected mass ﬂow and ˙mc,ref
is the corrected ﬂow design point. Additionally, to ﬁnd the relative corrected compressor speed lines that go
across the contour plot in the operation are can be written as [20]:
Nc,r = Ncorp
Nref
(52)
34
==PAGE:45==
Where Nc,r is the non-dimension corrected relative compressor speed, Ncor is the corrected compressor speed and
Nc,ref is the corrected compressor speed design point. The pressure side of the compressor map is represented
with a relative reduced pressure ratio. The equation for the relative reduced pressure ratio that forms the y-axis
of the compressor map can be written as [20]:
Pr,re =
Pr,t −1
Pr,ref −1
(53)
Where Pr,re is the relative reduced pressure ratio, Pr,t is the actual pressure ratio and Pr,ref is the design
pressure ratio. The pressure ratio is found by stagnation pressure levels. The relative eﬃciency is the ratio of
the actual eﬃciency to the design eﬃciency. The calculative relative eﬃciency is the foundation of the contour
eﬃciency islands. The equation for the relative eﬃciency can be written as [20]:
ηr =
η
ηref
(54)
Where ηr is the relative eﬃciency, η is the actual eﬃciency and ηref is the design eﬃciency.
The contour function is used to create the compressor map. The contour plot displays isolines of Z matrix in the
x-y plane. The Z matrix contains information about the isentropic eﬃciency. In order to create the Z matrix,
the function griddata was applied. Griddata interpolates 2-D or 3-D scattered data based on the values that
are given to the function. As a result, with interpolation, a compressor map can be created without having
data for all conditions. The mass ﬂow and pressure ratio are rearranged in incremental order and turned into
2-D coordinates via meshgrid function in Matlab. The boundary plot area was found by the surge line, choke
line, highest speed line, and the lowest speed line. The function inpolygon returns a binary matrix indicating
whether the points are located inside or on the edge of the polygonal region. The values of the interpolated grid
data function are erased if they don not match the boundary area. Thus, keeping the eﬃciency islands inside
the boundary area.
The surge line and the choke line are plotted on top of the contour plot. The surge line values are selected from
each valid speed line based on the minimum mass ﬂow and the highest pressure ratio found, that is to say, the
maximum achievable closure of the valve. The same procedure applies to the choke line however selected the
elements with the highest mass ﬂow and the lowest pressure ratio found. Those values are then matched for
the pressure ratio and mass ﬂow by indexing. Thereafter, the values are extracted to a surge and choke lines
vector to form the plot lines.
Additionally, the speed curves are added to the contour map demonstrating the relationship between the com-
pressor speed, mass ﬂow, pressure, and eﬃciency.
The compressor map is later on smoothed out to create a more malleable or a fancier map. Smoothing the
compressor map is a common practice when making a compressor map. However, it should be noted that a
smoother map is less accurate. In order to smoothen out the eﬃciency islands on the map, the function gridﬁt.m
was applied by John D’Errico. The function creates a smooth surface that approximates the data while letting
the user stays in control of the smoothness. The built-in functions polyﬁt and polyval were used to make the
speed, the surge and the choke curves smoother. Polyﬁt creates the polynomial equations of a desired order
and polyval is a function that returns the output values based on polyﬁt and independent input variable.
35
==PAGE:46==
3
Results And Discussions
This section displays results of data from the experiment, ﬁnding from the calculations and the constructed
compressor maps. The subsections follow the resulting order.
3.1
Input data
There is always a change that problems can arise when conducting an experiment. The procedure as described
in Planning of experiments 2.2 could not be brought to a complete ﬁnish due to an issue with the cooling system
and water leakage into the gearbox cooling system that eventually shut down the experiment process before
its completion. Additionally, controlling the throttle valve position according to the planning was impossible.
The throttle valve position could only be determined by visual observation on a fainted gauge onto the throttle
valve. This section, therefore, shows only attainable data for some of the compressor speeds and valve positions
from the initial planning.
All data from the experiments are plotted in ﬁgures 25 and 26. The ﬁgures show the complete data course for
inlet- and outlet temperatures and pressure, along with the impeller speed on the 15th of May and on the 18th
of May, respectively.
Figure 25: Complete data summary from the 15th of May.
In both ﬁgures, the x-axis represents measurement timing, the left y-axis represents the working ﬂuid tem-
perature in degrees Celsius or the pressure in kilopascal (kPa) depending on the parameter. The right y-axis
represents the compressor’s impeller speed in rounds per minute. These graphs reveal the development of the
experiment as a function of time and help to select time intervals for further examination.
36
==PAGE:47==
Figure 26: Complete data summary from the 18th of May.
It is safe to say that the plots reveal great ﬂuctuations in the system that should operate in a stable manner.
However, when looked closer, there are small intervals that show a negligible or a minor rate of change simulta-
neously for all parameters. From ﬁgure 25, the intervals for the compressor speeds of 58,500 rpm, 72,000 rpm,
and 81,000 rpm ran with under constant compressor speed with changing valve positions. Therefore, making
the interval under those compressor speeds valid for further inspection. The same applied to the compressor
speed intervals of 49,500 rpm and 58,500 rpm in ﬁgure 26. Within those speeds intervals were short intervals
showing a negligible or a minor rate of change simultaneously for all parameters. These intervals were tested for
stability as described in Data selection and import 2.3.1. The collection of all the interval tables and constant
compressor speed ﬁgures used to derive the input parameters are found the appendix for the sake of a good
presentation.

The ﬁndings from table 3 are at stagnation conditions. The mass ﬂow rate ˙m ranges from 0.038 to 0.144 kg/s.
Overall, the mass ﬂow decreases with a changed throttle valve position and that was excepted. In addition,
an increase in the compressor speed results in a higher mass ﬂow rate with a fully open throttle valve except
for N = 72, 000 rpm. In comparison to N = 81, 000 rpm, with a fully open valve in both cases, a higher
mass ﬂow rate is found for N = 72, 000 rpm. That is surprising since the recorded pressure drop is higher
for N = 81, 000 rpm. From table 2, the same pattern is noticeable. For a reminder, the diﬀerential sensor is
installed in the discharge line. Inconsistency in the inlet conditions results in a lower pressure in the discharge
line for N = 81, 000 rpm than for N = 72, 000 rpm according to table 1. For other speeds, the pressure in the
discharge line increases with a higher compressor speed. Eﬀects on density are also noticed. The mass ﬂow
equation is correlated to the value of pressure and density. Increasing pressure and density increase the mass
ﬂow. Other parameters recorded, such a volumetric ﬂow and ﬂuid velocity follow a trend to the compressor
speed. Therefore, this inconsistency can be caused by various inlet conditions.
The volumetric ﬂow rate was found on both sides. As the mass ﬂow rate is constant over the compressor for
each condition, the volumetric ﬂow rate decreases as the ﬂuid density increases. The volumetric ﬂow rate is en-
chanted consistently with higher compressor speed. That applies also to the ﬂuid velocity as the cross-sectional
area is ﬁxed. Regarding the presentation of ﬂuid velocities on the suction line, the cause of that is a diﬀerent
cross-sectional area where the pressure and the temperature sensors are installed. The dynamic pressure and the
dynamic temperature are dependent on the ﬂuid velocity, therefore the velocities had to be found. The diameter
of the suction pipe where the temperature sensor is installed is the same for the discharge. The diﬀerence in
the ﬂuid velocities VS,T and VD is as result of the diﬀerence in the volumetric ﬂow rate over the compressor.
The main ﬁnding regarding the density is as mentioned before the inconsistency of the ﬂuid density evolution
at the compressor speed N = 81, 000 rpm in comparison to other compressor speed level.

The static temperature is now lower in table 4 compared to the recorded temperature values by the temperature
sensor. As explained in the methodology of stagnation conditions 2.3.3 the temperature sensors were measuring
part of the dynamic temperature. This does not apply to the pressure sensors.
The dynamic temperature and pressure levels are correlated to the ﬂuid velocity and its static conditions.
Therefore, the dynamic values grow with the ﬂuid velocity and the static conditions. Overall, the magnitude
of the dynamic conditions to the static stagnation is small. However, a minor change in the temperature or
pressure level can be very noticeable for the isentropic eﬃciency of the compressor.

The heat loss varies with diﬀerent the stagnation temperatures through the sightseeing glass, the heat losses
are from 0.08 kW to 0.11 kW. Speciﬁc enthalpy drop ∆hS is dependent on the mass ﬂow, lower the mass ﬂow
higher the speciﬁc enthalpy drop for a given heat loss. Speciﬁc enthalpy losses ∆hS range from 0.08 to 2.16 kJ/kg
The heat loss is in general not signiﬁcant in degrees or in kilowatts but other the other hand the eﬀects are
worthy of attention in terms of the isentropic eﬃciency, especially in the points that show a high isentropic
eﬃciency. Consequently, the point found to have the highest isentropic eﬃciency, without heat loss aﬀects the
isentropic eﬃciency is ηis = 0, 909 and is decreased to ηis = 0, 884 by heat loss eﬀects. As the temperature
diﬀerence increases over the compressor the eﬀects of heat loss on isentropic eﬃciency diminishes. The decrease
in isentropic eﬃciency runs from as little of 0.2% up to 3,3 %.

0.71
The isentropic eﬃciency ranges from 41 to 88%. There is an almost pure consistency that a lower mass ﬂow
results in a higher isentropic eﬃciency in comparison to a higher mass ﬂow among harmonious speed curves
except for the variant at 49.500 rpm, with a mass ﬂow of ˙m = 0.059 kg/s and with an isentropic eﬃciency of
ηis = 0.88. It would be interesting to see how the performance would evolve for more data and to see whether
if the maximum isentropic eﬃciency found would remain as the peak performance.
The most interesting ﬁnding from table 6 is the high isentropic eﬃciency of the compressor. Where the com-
pressor performance is peaking at ηis = 0.88 and as a result is greater than the design point isentropic eﬃciency
of ηis,design = 0.745. Additionally, there are several points that have an isentropic eﬃciency greater than the
design isentropic eﬃciency. The compressor’s design point was intended for air as a working ﬂuid but with
minor changes on the compressor, it can run with steam. This raises a question of the causes behind that and
the answer to that is multifold.
The ﬁrst factor being that operating with steam can simply result in a higher isentropic eﬃciency than antici-
pated with air. The second factor is the uncertainty of the isentropic eﬃciency, that is based on instrumental
accuracy, dynamic conditions, and heat losses. The uncertainty is large in most cases, especially at high isen-
tropic eﬃciency value as will be demonstrated in table 7. By withdrawing the uncertainty from the isentropic
eﬃciency it still leaves one point above the design point eﬃciency, that is ηis = 88.4 %. The third factor can be
heat loss, if so then most likely as a minor contributor. The calculation is an approximation as the scope glass
is assumed to be diabatic and the duct to be adiabatic. However, the heat loss uncertainty was set at a high
value and is included in the isentropic eﬃciency uncertainty to account for uncertainty. The fourth factor is the
data instability that can have a large impact on the compressor’s isentropic eﬃciency and may be exaggerating
the performance. The ﬁfth factor can be air inﬁltration into the system when the working ﬂuid pressure level
falls below atmospheric pressure. A minor amount of air in the working ﬂuid composition when enhance the
isentropic eﬃciency. This is shown in a previous experiment done by Weel & Sandvig [20].
The causes of the high isentropic eﬃciency recorded in the experiment can, therefore, be a cause of one or
several factors listed here above.
42
==PAGE:53==
3.7
Uncertainty results

5.12
The total isentropic eﬃciency uncertainty is in general signiﬁcant, ranging from 3,66 to 11,08 %. Especially, in
terms of the coeﬃcient of variant, the volatility is high, ranging from 5,12 to 23,53 %. The sensitivity due to
temperature error is higher at lower speeds, mainly with contributions from the suction line temperature. The
reason behind that is higher error eﬀects by heat losses and the partial scale of temperature error is larger at a
lower temperature.The pressure error has a stronger impact on the isentropic eﬃciency at lower speeds.
Moreover, the suction line temperature and pressure error contribute consistently more to the performance
uncertainty than the discharge side. The dynamic temperature and pressure uncertainty grow with increased
mass ﬂow rate within each constant compressor speed. Moreover, the heat loss through the sightseeing glass
adds to the temperature uncertainty on the suction side throughout the data collection. However, even when
testing the data for the same value of uncertainty on both sides, the suction line contributes more to the overall
uncertainty as the magnitude of the uncertainty is a bigger portion of the total temperature and pressure value.
Another factor is the uncertainty eﬀects on the compressor map. For the large uncertainties found, make the
course that the isentropic eﬃciency lines take on the compressor map more questionable.
It is relevant to mention the uncertainty of a possible air inﬁltration into the system. It appears if the working
ﬂuid is mixed with a minor amount of air it can enhance the compressor performance [20]. However, there are
no quantitative data over proportionality of air in the system and thus no way of knowing if air is combined
with the working ﬂuid. Therefore, the uncertainty by air inﬁltration is not accounted for.
What the uncertainty does is to indicate the range which the true performance of the compressor is the most
likely to be within. Consequently, when taking that into consideration, the scale of the uncertainties recorded,
especially around the peak points, makes it more diﬃcult to choose an operating point with a certainty. In other
words, as a result of a high uncertainty, it creates doubts whether if the compressor will deliver an isentropic
eﬃciency close to the desired value. Furthermore, a typical value for the isentropic performance ranges from
75 to 85 % [13]. On that ground, it is doubtful that the true isentropic performance for the peak points is
found far upwards the uncertainty range. Overall, the isentropic eﬃciency uncertainty is too great at lower
compressor speed but reaches more reasonable levels at higher speeds, notably for 72,000 rpm and above were
43
==PAGE:54==
the performance uncertainty falls under 5 %.

Figure 27: Corrected water vapor compressor map.
Figure 27 shows a corrected compressor map of the compressor. The corrected mass ﬂow is on the x-axis and
the pressure ratio on the y-axis. In the upper left corner are the design point conditions. The qmref and Nref
are the design mass ﬂow and the compressor design speed at standard conditions, respectively. On the other
hand, Prref is the actual design pressure and etaref is the actual design isentropic eﬃciency. In the upper right
corner of the graph is the another label box. The ﬁrst label is the surge line and appears as the upper red line
in the graph. The choke line is the lower red line. The black lines that travel across the contour plot connecting
the surge and the choke lines are the corrected compressor speed curves. Their value is denoted on the plot.
It is necessary to mention that the corrected speed curves are not entirely correct. The reason behind that is
inconsistent inlet conditions for the relevant groups of data. Consequently are the compressor corrected speed
curves an average of slightly diﬀerent values under a constant actual speed. This can be seen in table 9. The
diﬀerent color lines/islands are the contour lines and represent the compressor’s isentropic eﬃciency.
46
==PAGE:57==
Figure 28: Corrected water vapor compressor map with data points.
Figure 28 is the same compressor map as the one shown by ﬁgure 27. However, the data points used to form
the map are plotted onto the map to see how they align with the isentropic eﬃciency trajectories.
All of the contour lines or the isentropic eﬃciency lines are accurate in that sense that data points fall between
the correct contour lines or onto them. Therefore, the validation is successful.
The area of lower data point density appears to have to be less dense, notably the area around isentropic eﬃ-
ciency lines of 70% and 75% in above the second lowest speed curve. The reason behind that is not necessarily
the cause of few data points. The isentropic eﬃciency might be changing less in that area but it is hard to
say for sure. The best way of knowing that for sure is to make up the compressor map with more data points.
Because the performance lines are created with an interpolation, more data points would aﬀect their the path
of their curves, hence making the compressor map more accurate. Probably, would more data points aﬀect the
interpolation more in areas currently less dense.
The choke line in the compressor map is showing the maximum mass ﬂow rate for each compressor speed
recorded. A compressor is more likely to enter choke conditions at higher compressor speeds hence at a higher
ﬂuid velocity. It is ambiguous if choke conditions were achieved since the compressor speed curves do not show
a vertical slop. With a vertical speed curve slop, the pressure ratio over the compressor would decrease and
the ﬂuid velocity would increase under a constant mass ﬂow rate and the compressor would have choked as
explained in Surge and choke 1.5.7. An possible explanation why choke conditions would not be achieved are
pressure losses in the system (pipes, valves and so on), forcing the compressor to maintain a minimum pressure
diﬀerence. However, it is possible that long distance between measurement points are generating a visual error.
The compressor speed line in the compressor map would ideally be vertical to show a surge condition. It is a
bit ambiguous when looking at the compressor whether surge is obtained as a results of long distances between
the data points close to the surge line. However, during the experiment the throttle valve position was altered
to a maximum closure achievable hence the surge line should be in order.
Overall accuracy of the compressor map then testing the position of the dataset is good. That is not surprising
since the interpolation should take into account the dataset values. What is harder to predict is how a new
stable dataset tested under the same conditions would align in the map, this applies to all of the compressor
maps generated in this paper. For example under other compressor speeds than tested for and obverse how
they match with the performance trajectories. However, if there would exist more stable data intervals to test
47
==PAGE:58==
with they would have been used to enhance the compressor map. In general, in regards to all of the compressor
maps, having a larger data collection to make up the compressor map gives the maps a higher accuracy. Not
only in regards to the isentropic eﬃciency lines but for the speed lines, the surge line and the choke line. More-
over, information for the operational area below the recorded maximum eﬃciency is missing, it would be very
interesting to see how the trajectories would evolve under that area. Similarly, there are no information for the
maximum speed of the compressor.

layouts of the compressor map are the same as introduced before.

==PAGE:59==
Figure 30 displays the same smoothed corrected compressor map as the one shown by ﬁgure 29. However, the
data points used to form the map are plotted onto the map to see how they align with the curves. The curves
in the smoothed compressor map conversion are considerably smoother compared to ﬁgure 27. Additionally,
the isentropic eﬃciency trajectories are moving diﬀerently along their path, most noticeably the ηis = 0.75
trajectory that moves a bit back and forth.
Figure 30: Smooth corrected water vapor compressor map with data points.
Moreover, the compressor speed of Nc = 47, 661 rpm, is linear because of its polynomial of any degree was out of
bound. Except for that particular curve, the data points align themselves closer to the speed curve they belong
to. Other speed lines, surge line, and the discharge line are polynomials of second order. The surge line for
Nc = 33, 755 rpm is extensively increasing the safe operational area, meanwhile the surge line for Nc = 39, 444
rpm is indicating a narrower operational area. The same applies to the choke line. When the data points fall to
the right of the trajectory, then the choke line is indicating that the possible mass ﬂow rate is less than actually
and vice versa for data points to the left of the trajectory, indicating a larger mass ﬂow rate than actually
possible.
Although the corrected compressor map is smoothened and errors are expected, all of the data points fall neatly
in their place. The errors found are related to the operational area and the speed curves on a small-scale. In
light of this, the overall accuracy is acceptable.

==PAGE:65==
4
Conclusions And Recommendations
4.1
Conclusions
The objective of the project 1.2 was to create a corrected compressor map and corrected relative compressor
map based on the test rig results. In light of results found, the following conclusions are made.
The corrected compressor map and the corrected relative compressor map were both constructed and were
successfully validated, thereby fulﬁlling the objectives.
There are a few limitation to the compressor maps because of unmanageable problems with the cooling system
causing a lack of a suﬃcient amount of usable data. This has several impacts on the compressor maps accuracy:
• The operational area displayed by the compressor maps spans a smaller range than feasible for the com-
pressor.
• Another factor is the certainty of the trajectories that improves as the functional dataset gets larger. As
the accuracy gets better then validation for data alien to the interpolation gets more accurate. Currently,
it is ambiguous by looking at the compressor maps whether surge and choke conditions have been achieved.
Moreover, the compressor speed curves should with better data round more naturally. The long distance
between the data points under constant speed makes the compressor speed curves more linear.
The
isentropic eﬃciency lines are likely be more accurate in their path with more data, notably in an area less
dense of data points.
• Thirdly, although precautions were made, there is a possibility that the data collection is to some extent
unstable and thereby aﬀecting the performance calculations. For instance, the high isentropic eﬃciencies
found in comparison to the expected maximum eﬃciency of η = 0.745 can be caused by operational
instability.
The parameters that make up the compressor map have inherent some degree of uncertainty. The main issue is
the large uncertainty of isentropic eﬃciency at lower speeds. Therefore, those isentropic eﬃciency trajectories
have a high uncertainty.
Overall accuracy of the compressor maps can, therefore, be improved by getting a more stable data collection
and decreasing uncertainty.
A smoothed version of the compressor maps was generated. Although the errors are expected when generating a
smoothed version, the data points fall neatly in their place and the errors found are on a small-scale. Therefore,
the smoothed compressor maps have an satisfactory level of accuracy to their initial maps.
55
==PAGE:66==
4.2
Recommendations
The cooling system was a large limiting factor in the experiment. The main recommendation is to get the cooling
system in order and thus being able to build a more detailed compressor map that spans a larger operational
range. Weel & Sandvig is aware of this and has already initiated a process to improve the cooling system for
future work in relation to the heat pump development.
Moreover, with increased operational stability, it would be interesting to validate current ﬁndings, for instance,
the high performance detected in the results. In addition to that, the compressor maps themselves could also
be revalidated with new data.
The uncertainties found in the experiment are too great for many of the data points. Installation of more
accurate temperature and pressure instruments would result in a lower isentropic eﬃciency uncertainty.
Another recommendation is to improve the control over the throttle valve in order to make a systematic ap-
proach to the experiment more manageable.
Moving the suction line temperature sensor behind the sightseeing glass, thus behind the heat loss area, would
lead to a less heat loss uncertainty since the duct is overall considered to be adiabatic.
Correcting the calibration for the diﬀerential pressure sensor and the suction line pressure sensor is recom-
mended.
Having an instrument to measure the ﬂuid composition would improve the uncertainty calculations.

  1 %% Compressor efficiency uncertainty 
  2 
  3 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
  4 %     Init_FluidProp     %
  5 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
  6 
  7 
  8 global FP
  9 
 10 %Create an instance of the FluidProp COM server
 11 FP = actxserver ('FluidProp.FluidProp');
 12 
 13 Msg='FluidProp: COM object created';
 14 disp(Msg)
 15 
 16 nCmp  = 1;         % of no importance
 17 Cnc   = [1,0];     % of no importance
 18 Model = 'IF97';
 19 Cmp   = 'steam';
 20 
 21 ErrorMsg = invoke(FP,'SetFluid_M',Model,nCmp,Cmp,Cnc)
 22 
 23 %% Independent variables
 24 
 25 %create a function for the proporational dp error
 26 dp_drop=[0 0.0029 0.0147 0.0352 0.0475 0.0597 0.0812 0.1038]; %the magnitude of 
pressure drop
 27 dp_drop_error=[0 0.0006 0.0037 0.0042 0.0055 0.0062 0.0082 0.0108]; 
%the magnitude 
of pressure drop error
 28 
 29 p_delta_dP = polyfit(dp_drop,dp_drop_error,2); 
 30 dp_drop_x= linspace(min(dp_drop),max(dp_drop),length(P_S));
 31 delta_dP= polyval(p_delta_dP,dp_drop_x);
 32 
 33 for i=1:length(P_S)
 34 
 35 
 36 delta_T_S_1(1,i)=0.75+T_S_1_dynamic(1,i)*0.2+heat_loss_T_S(1,i)*0.2; 
% In degrees 
suction line
 37 delta_T_D_1(1,i)=0.75+T_D_1_dynamic(1,i)*0.2+heat_loss_T_D(1,i)*0.2; 
% In degrees 
discharge line
 38 delta_P_S(1,i)=0.0048+P_S_dynamic(1,i)*0.2; % In bara suction line
 39 delta_P_D(1,i)=0.0013+P_D_dynamic(1,i)*0.2; % In bara discharge line
 40 
 41 delta_C(1,i)=0.0045; %Uncertainty of Coefficient of discharge
 42 delta_epsilon(1,i)=0.00102; %Uncertainty of Expansibility [expansion] factor
 43 delta_D(1,i)=0.00215; %Uncertainty of the discharge pipe
 44 delta_d(1,i)=0.00263; %Uncertainty of the discharge pipe
 45 
 46 %create a function for the proporational dp error
 47 
 48 
 49 % delta_dP(1,i)=0.00263; %Uncertainty of the pressure difference over the orifice
 50 delta_rho_d(1,i)=sqrt((delta_T_D_1(1,i)/T_D_1_stag(1,i))^2+(delta_P_D(1,i)
/P_D_stag(1,i))^2); %Uncertainty of the density
==PAGE:87==

2 of 3
 51 
 52 delta_q_m(1,i)=q_m(1,i)*sqrt((delta_C(1,i))^2+(delta_epsilon(1,i))^2+(((2*beta(1,
i)^4)/(1-beta(1,i)^4))^2)*(delta_D(1,i))^2+(((2)/(1-beta(1,i)^4))^2)*(delta_d(1,i))
^2+0.25*(delta_dP(1,i))^2+0.25*(delta_rho_d(1,i)^2));
 53 delta_q_m_percentage(1,i)=(delta_q_m(1,i)/q_m(1,i))*100; 
 54 
 55 T_S_1_error(1,i)=delta_T_S_1(1,i)+T_S_1(1,i)+T_S_1_dynamic(1,i); 
%deg
 56 T_D_1_error(1,i)=delta_T_D_1(1,i)+T_D_1(1,i)+T_D_1_dynamic(1,i); 
%deg
 57 P_S_error(1,i)=delta_P_S(1,i)+P_S(1,i)+P_S_dynamic(1,i);% bar
 58 P_D_error(1,i)=delta_P_D(1,i)+P_D(1,i)+P_D_dynamic(1,i);% bar
 59 
 60 i=i+1;
 61 end

 63 for i=1:length(P_S)
 64  [h_s_error_TS1(1,i),ErrorMsg] = invoke(FP,'Enthalpy','PT', P_S_stag(1,i), 
T_S_1_error(1,i)); %kJ/kg
 65  [s_s_error_TS1(1,i),ErrorMsg] = invoke(FP,'Entropy','PT', P_S_stag(1,i), 
T_S_1_error(1,i)); %kJ/kgK
 66  [h_d_error_TS1(1,i),ErrorMsg] = invoke(FP,'Enthalpy','PT', P_D_stag(1,i), 
T_D_1_stag(1,i)); %kJ/kg
 67  [h_d_is_error_TS1(1,i),ErrorMsg] = invoke(FP,'Enthalpy','sP', s_s_error_TS1(1,i), 
P_D_stag(1,i)); %kJ/kgK
 68  eta_error_TS1(1,i)=(h_d_is_error_TS1(1,i)-h_s_error_TS1(1,i))/(h_d_error_TS1(1,i)
-h_s_error_TS1(1,i))-eta_stag(1,i); %isentropic efficiency error
 69  
 70  [h_s_error_TD1(1,i),ErrorMsg] = invoke(FP,'Enthalpy','PT', P_S_stag(1,i),

 71  [s_s_error_TD1(1,i),ErrorMsg] = invoke(FP,'Entropy','PT', P_S_stag(1,i),

 323 Kelvin_addition=273.15; % Kelvin
 324 T_air=23; %deg
 325 k_stain=16.2; % thermal conduction of stainless steel [W/m*K]
 326 k_glass=1.14; % thermal conduction of sightseeing glass  [W/m*K]
 327 h_steam=15000; %thermal convection of steam in a pipe [W/m^2*K]
 328 velocoty_air=2; %minimum velocity, can range from 2 to 20 [m/s]
 329 h_air=10-velocoty_air+10*velocoty_air^0.5; % thermal convection of air [W/m^2*K]
 330 r_a=0.5*72.9/1000; %inner radius at stainless steel [m]
 331 r_b=0.5*76.2/1000; %outer radius at stainless steel [m]
 332 r_d=0.5*75.4/1000; %inner radius at sightseeing glass [m]
 333 r_e=0.5*85/1000; %outer radius at sightseeing glass [m]
 334 L_glass=80/1000; % length of the sightseeing glass [m]
 335 L_total=162/1000; % total length [m]
 336 L_stain=L_total-L_glass; % length of the stainless steel [m]
 337 r_tot_steel=(1/(2*h_steam*pi*r_a*L_stain))+((log(r_b/r_a))/
(2*pi*k_stain*L_stain))+(1/(2*h_air*pi*r_b*L_stain)); %thermal resistance [K/W]
 338 r_tot_glass=(1/(2*h_steam*pi*r_d*L_glass))+((log(r_e/r_d))/
(2*pi*k_glass*L_glass))+(1/(2*h_air*pi*r_e*L_glass)); %thermal resistance [K/W]
 339 U_over=1/r_tot_steel+1/r_tot_glass; %Overall heat transer coefficient
 340 U_glass=1/r_tot_glass; %Overall heat transer coefficient
 341 U_stain=1/r_tot_steel; %Overall heat transer coefficient
 342 
 343 %% Heat losses
 344 
 345 for i=1:length(P_S)
 346 
 347 q_r(1,i)=(U_over)*(T_S_1_stag(1,i)-T_air)/(0.9*1000); %heat transer [kW]
 348 heat_loss_h_s(1,i)=1*q_r(1,i)/q_m(1,i); %the enthalpy transfered
 349 heat_loss_h_d(1,i)=0*q_r(1,i)/q_m(1,i); %the enthalpy transfered
 350 heat_loss_h(1,i)=heat_loss_h_s(1,i)+heat_loss_h_d(1,i); %the total enthalpy 
transfered
 351 
 352 new_h_s(1,i)=h_s(1,i)-heat_loss_h_s(1,i); %enthaplpy with the heat loss kJ/kg
 353 new_h_d(1,i)=h_d(1,i)+heat_loss_h_d(1,i); %enthaplpy with the heat loss kJ/kg

i)); %deg
